==PAGE:5==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
iii 
 
The purpose of this Master’s Thesis was to evaluate the dynamic response of a highly irregularly 
Vancouver style high-rise podium tower, by investigating the influence of the floor diaphragm 
assumptions. The current research on the behaviour of floor diaphragm concentrates in symmetrical 
buildings with multiple shear walls. However, research on horizontal floor diaphragms are missing 
regarding irregular high-rise buildings with a single concrete core; it is just not known how accurate 
the real performance is captured both in experimental and in numerical analysis. Hence, this research 
project aimed to enhance the understanding of the horizontal behaviour of high-rise buildings, by 
investigating the movement of the horizontal diaphragms and performing a linear dynamic analysis 
of the storey diaphragms.  
 
The performed Ambient Vibration Test was during construction for the 32 – storey reinforces 
concrete building in downtown Vancouver, British Columbia Canada. Identification of the dynamic 
parameters as frequencies, damping ratios and mode shape of the high-rise by performing Operational 
Modal Analysis. The study includes building two comprehensive linear Finite Element Models from 
the structural drawings of the podium tower, one model with the six-storey basement and one without 
the basement. The first 12 mode shapes of the high-rise were estimated with the Operational Modal 
Analysis method, and matched with the estimated frequencies and mode shapes from the Finite 
Element Models. The Finite Element Model without the basement showed a difference in frequency 
of maximum 7 % for the longitudinal North-South and East-West mode shapes and 16 % for the 
torsional mode shape. The results of the Finite Element Model with the basement showed a frequency 
difference increase of 15 % for the longitudinal modes and no significant difference for the torsional 
mode shape. Both Finite Element Models had excellent Modal Assurance Criterions values of 90 % 
for the first six mode shapes. 
 
The assumptions for the floor diaphragm of this structure had a significant influence on the global 
structural response. For the Finite Element Model without the basement modelling of the floor 
diaphragms as flexible gave an absolute difference of 6 – 16 %, for the frequencies for the first three 
modes compared to the experimental results. The rigid diaphragm assumption introduced a difference 
in the frequency response of 42 - 54 % compared to the experimental results. The observed modal 
assurance criterion for each mode shape was significantly higher when assuming the flexible or semi-
==PAGE:6==
Abstract 
 
iv 
 
rigid diaphragms compared to the rigid diaphragm assumption. It is evident for this irregular single 
core high-rise building assuming rigid diaphragms overestimates the global frequency response 
significantly, and it not applicable to this type of structures. By performing modal updating of 
different parameters for the FE-Model the frequencies and mode shapes were enhanced.

v 
 
I owe special thanks to Dr Freddy Pina for his belief in this project and the work he carried out to 
establish the collaboration with The University of British Columbia (UBC) and the Technical 
University of Denmark (DTU). His guidance and knowledge from the consultant engineering industry 
were essential for the Finite Element Modelling. 
 
I thank Assistant Professor Evangelos Katasnos my principal supervisor at DTU for endorsing the 
project and always sharing his enthusiasm about the project and the research. With his guidance and 
support throughout the studies, I managed to achieve an extensiveness quantity and quality of work, 
which I had never considered possible for a master thesis. Assistant Professor Evangelos Katasnos 
had confidence in my skills and his contribution to the financial founding was priceless. 
   
I owe special thanks to Professor Carlos Ventura my supervisor at UBC, for endorsing the project 
and providing all the necessary equipment I needed during my stay at UBC. Professor Carlos Ventura 
ensured guidance and support during my two months at UBC, and he saw me as a close family friend, 
for which I am humble. I am honoured to have been a member of his research team in the Earthquake 
Engineering Research Facility at UBC.  
  
I thank Postdoc Sandro D. R. Amador my co-supervisor at DTU for valuable guidance about 
Operational Modal Analysis and updating for the Finite Element Model. He always opened his office 
to me to come and work, and the results of the updating part show his interstress in the study.  
 
I thank Professor Rune Brincker my co-supervisor at DTU for his much-appreciated advice and 
comments on the results of the study. His experience within Operational Modal Analysis and Finite 
Element Model updating contributed beneficially to the quality of the research. 
 
Special thanks to my beloved parents MD PhD. Anette and MD PhD. Lars for always supporting me 
and listen to my challenges. I am grateful for their financial contribution to my studies abroad and the 
constant belief in my skills, and I admire their mindset of excellent results achieved through extensive 
hard work. I thank my brother, Peter for his support and guidance within electrical instruments. I owe

I thank my beloved girlfriend, Noushin for the unconditional support and belief in my research. She 
became my family in during my studies at UBC and carried for me from the first day without 
expecting anything in return. She always gave me advice reminded me to enjoy my research time too. 
 
I thank my fellow research students PhD student Alexander Mendler and PhD candidate Ilaria

research-related discussions and socials events made the difference between being absent from my 
family. I am grateful for the advise and comment on my thesis and support. 
 
I thank the staff of EERF for allowing me to participate and performing ambient vibration test of

assistance with recoding of the converting program

==PAGE:19==
Introduction 
 
1 
 
 
A large number of existing structures as buildings, bridges and other infrastructures have decreased 
performance, and it is very uncertain to predict the capacity with confidence for the rest of the service 
life. Based on this, it is a high priority for the engineering society to identify the real performance of 
structures when they are exposed to different types of effects. This study shows a way to precisely 
predict the current capacity of the structure, and evaluate the opportunity for retrofitting, if necessary. 
Primary there is a gap in the literature related to studies concentrated on horizontal movements of 
diaphragms, and this incomplete knowledge has already lead to simplified models, which cannot 
predict the precise horizontal movements.   
 
1.1 Problem Statement 
Simplified assumptions are accounted for when modelling the diaphragm performance of buildings; 
but we are missing how accurate the real performance is captured both in experimental and numerical 
studies, to identify a refine modelling approached for diaphragm performance.  
How can we reinforce the refine diaphragm modelling to capture the real performance? 
 
1.2 Objectives, Scope and Time Plan 
The objectives of this study are understanding of the horizontal behaviour of high-rise buildings, by 
investigating the movement of the horizontal diaphragms and performing a linear dynamic analysis 
of the storey diaphragms. The emphasis of the project is to clarify the diaphragm effect about 
horizontal behaviour in high-rise buildings.  
 
This master project aimed to contribute to the literature, regarding the influence of the horizontal 
diaphragm assumptions of existing reinforced concrete structures exposed to wind loads, earthquake 
loads and ambient vibrations. How do the diaphragm assumptions influence the global structural 
response, and are our Finite Element Models capturing the real structural response?  
 
1.2.1 The Scopes of This Study: 
1. Investigation of the diaphragm effect composes acquiring ambient accelerations data from the 
horizontal movement of a high-rise building, located in Vancouver Canada.  
==PAGE:20==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
2 
 
2. Comparison of the obtained ambient data with a comprehensive linear 3D Finite Element 
Model of the high-rise building.  
3. Identification of the dynamic properties, as modes and Eigenfrequencies, of the acquired 
ambient accelerations with Operational Modal Analysis and the 3D FE Model. 
4. A comprehensive comparison of the captured diaphragm effect with the level of detailing of 
the diaphragms in the 3D FE model.   
 
1.2.2 Time Plane for The Master’s Project: 
- 
1st of August to 30th of September (2 months) at UBC in Vancouver, objectives for the stay 
were to acquire acceleration data of the selected high-rise buildings and performed operational 
modal analysis to identify the dynamic properties of the structure. Start of modelling the 
structure in a Finite Element program from the building plan drawings. 
- 
1st of October to 22nd of January (4 months) at DTU, continued structure modelling in the 
Finite Element program. Identification of the dynamic properties and comparing the 3D FE 
Model with the acquired acceleration data. The final part of the study includes a comparison 
of the diaphragm effect versus the level of assumptions. 
 
 
1.3 Current research  
When modelling the floor diaphragm, it is vital to take the in-plane flexibility and deformation into 
consideration. The in-plan flexibility has been studied several times with quantitative analysis of 
multiple buildings, to clarify trends for floor diaphragm regarding if the behaviour of the diaphragm 
is considered rigid or flexible.  Research published within this field of research is limited, and the 
following section is a summary of the current published research. 
Saffarini and Qudaimat [1], investigated 37 reinforced concrete structures, with a static analysis with 
a different number of stories, storey height, regularity of the building plan, number of columns and 
shear walls. The conclusion of the study was, for buildings without shear walls the rigid-floor 
diaphragm assumption is accurate, but for buildings with shear walls, the assumption is no longer 
valid.  
Ju and Lin [2], concludes shear walls can cause significant errors in the building response when the 
diaphragm is assumed rigid compared with the flexible assumption. The conducted quantitative 
investigation had 520 different buildings, with T-shaped, U-shaped and rectangular shaped buildings.  
==PAGE:21==
Introduction 
 
3 
 
Basu and Jain [3], investigate asymmetric buildings and the influence of flexibility of the floor 
diaphragm with particular attention to torsion. They concluded, for buildings with flexible floor 
diaphragms the torsional effect might be significant and can cause considerable errors. Their study 
further state in-plane floor deformation and torsion can pronely co-occur for buildings with horizontal 
offsets.  
Tena-Colunga and Abrams [4], investigates three existing flexible diaphragms buildings and compare 
the analytical results when the floor diaphragm is considered rigid or flexible. They find the flexible 
diaphragms experience higher displacements and accelerations than the rigid diaphragms. Increasing 
flexibility of the diaphragms reduces the torsional effect considerably, and buildings with flexible 
diaphragms have a considerably longer fundamental period than buildings with rigid diaphragms. 
Moeinin and Rafezy [5], investigates the structural responses of multiple reinforce concrete structures 
with shapes of T-shaped, U-shaped and rectangular. Through a comparative response spectrum 
dynamic analysis with several seismic buildings codes, to investigate the efficiency of the code 
provision. In general, dividing the codes into two categories regarding the diaphragms either the 
qualitative criteria related to the shape of the floor diaphragm (EC8, NZS4203 and GSC-2000) or the 
quantitative criteria related to the in-plane deformation (Iranian 2800, UBC 97, SEAOC-90 and 
FEMA-273). In general, the seismic codes accept modelling the diaphragm as simple 
beams/horizontal girders, with the web as the shear-resisting element as the deck, and the flanges 
reflect the chord or boundary element to resist the axial compression from the flexural action. The 
findings from this study criticise the seismic codes for being insufficient when the same building is 
considered rigid regarding the quantitative criteria but is considered flexible in the qualitative criteria. 
The author's further advice to use shell elements like the floor elements, due to the level of 
approximations and limitation with the beams or horizontal girders. The study concludes it is not 
suitable to have quantitative criteria depending on the in-plane deformation of the diaphragm since 
the deformation depends on the acting forces, which makes this criterion somewhat ambiguous.  
 
1.4 Conference Proceeding 
During the author’s stay at UBC, the author presented the preliminary results of the thesis at a three-
minute thesis presentation at the 1st China-Canada Symposium on Structural & Earthquake 
Engineering (CCSSEE), the conference three-minute thesis proceeding is attaching in Appendix A – 
conference proceeding.

This section is a summary of the essential theory in dynamics, for a more in-depth explanation of the 
theory of dynamics the author refers to other authors within this topic Chopra [6], Humar [7] and 
Patrick [8].  
 
2.1 Equation of Motion 
In every dynamic problem, the equation of motion (EOM) for the system is a central element. It is 
important to note; the EOM is an assumption of the behaviour of the real system. This section will 
shortly explain the derivation of the EOM for a single degree of freedom (SDOF) system, and extend 
it further to multi-degree of freedom system.  
 
2.1.1 Single Degree of Freedom System 
Nearly all dynamic engineering problems are multi-degree of freedom (MDOF) systems. Figure 1 
illustrates a braced frame system subjected to a force f, the system contains a mass for the floor, and 
vertical and later stiffness for the bracing and columns/walls. To describe the system as an SDOF 
system, its assumed the spring is massless, and the displacements are in the elastic range. Further, the 
vertical and the lateral stiffness are considered lumped. If the spring has a mass, it adds to the mass 
of the rigid body. Further damping of the system, is usually not a structural part of the system. Figure 
1 shows the SDOF system model of the braced frame system, and four forces act on the system, the 
external force f(𝑡), the elastic force f𝑠(𝑡) which resist deformation, the dissipative force f𝐷(𝑡) resist 
velocity and act opposite from the elastic force. The elastic and dissipative force are defined as 
follows. 
 
 
f𝑠(𝑡) = 𝑘𝑥(𝑡)       f𝐷(𝑡) = 𝑐𝑥̇(𝑡) 
 
(2.1) 
Where 𝑘 is the stiffness of the system, 𝑐 is the damping, 𝑥 is distance and 𝑥̇ is velocity.

acting in the opposite direction of the acceleration of the rigid body. This satisfies the SDOF system 
and the EOM is, [7].

2.2.3 Mode Superposition Method of Vibrating Systems 
For free and forced dynamics response for MDOF systems, a conventional approach to solve the 
MDOF systems is the mode superposition method. It is essential to note Equation (2.5) represents N 
simultaneous equations in the displacement vector 𝑥, and N corresponds to the degree of freedom. 
Solutions of the MDOF systems, is general solved by integrations methods, which is a mathematical 
approximation, meaning the solution of the system is an approximation. If an exact mathematical 
solutions of the MDOF systems is of interested, and the force function are represented as simple 
mathematical functions, it is beneficial to apply the mode superposition method. The superposition 
method defines a special set of coordinates, which is transformations of the equations, these are called 
modal coordinates (or natural coordinates). The transformation will uncouple the EOM, by that the 
system will be of N independent equations and not N dependents equations. The first step of the modal 
representation is the transformation. 
 
 
𝒙= 𝚽𝒚= ∑𝜙𝑛𝑦𝑛 
𝑁
𝑛=1
 
 
(2.26) 
Where 𝒚 the modal coordinates vector, 𝚽 the matrix of undamped mode shapes and 𝐱 the generalised 
coordinates vector. The subscript n indicates a specific place in the vector. Differentiation of the 
equation follows the first derivative of 𝐱̇ equals the mode shapes vector times the first derivative of 
the modal coordinates vector 𝐲̇, the same applies to the second derivative of  𝐱. Transposing the EOM 
in Equation (2.5) by substituting Equation (2.26) and premultiplied by 𝚽T, then equation is rewritten 
to 
 
 
𝚽T𝐌𝚽𝒚̈ + 𝚽T𝐂𝚽𝒚̇ + 𝚽T𝐊𝚽𝒚= 𝚽T𝐟 
 
(2.27) 
Both the mass and the stiffness matrix are diagonal due to the orthogonality property of the mode 
shapes, and if the damping matrix also has orthogonality property, Equation (2.27) becomes 
uncoupled and is a set of N equations describing the single modal responses. The total modal response 
of the system is a summation of each modal response, [7].

2.3 Dynamics of Diaphragms 
Modelling of floor diaphragms/slabs in multi-storey buildings are often as shells elements, and 
discretisation of the diaphragms can be considered massive computational power wise. To avoid 
massive computational models kinematic constraints are applied, and the constraints are often 
“master-slave” nodes for many automated structural analysis programs. A floor system in three 
dimensions with the shape of a rectangle and has four corner nodes. Each node has six degrees of 
freedom three translations and three rotations ones before any constraints are applied. If a structural 
element as columns or beams is attached to the floor system, this introduces additional 6 degrees of 
freedom per node. For large structures, the in-plane definition of the lower floors is often considered 
to be small compared with the horizontal inter-storey drift, and the applied master-slave approach 
will provide safe computation power, [9]. Reducing the degrees of freedom has disadvantages 
regarding the response of the floor system, by simplifying the behaviour of the floor, the response is 
simplified too, which for some structures lead to significant errors and non-conservative estimations, 
[3].   
 
Figure 3 illustrates the master-slave approach for the floor system, the centre of mass is located the 
point 𝑂 and a random point 𝑖 is considered.  By assuming the kinematic constraint the diaphragm is 
rigid, the displacement of any node can be expressed as linear function of the master node, in the 
point 𝑂. As the figures shows the displacements in the x-direction is illustrated as 𝑢𝑥
(𝑂), the 
corresponding y-direction is 𝑢𝑦
(𝑂) and the rotational displacement is illustrated as 𝑢𝜃𝑧
(𝑂).

2.4 Response Analysis of Multi-degree of Freedom Systems 
The modal response of a simple multi-degrees of freedom system often contains the full structural 
response within the first modes, when the structures become more complex and taller the question 
about how many modes to consider, is not always possible to see from the mode shape. The influence 
of higher mode response for tall structures can be significant, and the modal participation ratio is 
often used as an indicator to determine the number of modes, there has to be taken into account. The 
==PAGE:31==
Theory of Dynamics 
 
13 
 
modal mass participation ratio describes how much of the total modal mass participates in the given 
modes.  
 
 
Γ𝑛= 𝜙𝑛
𝑇𝐌𝑟
𝜙𝑛𝑇𝐌𝜙𝑛
 
 
(2.31) 
Where r is the influence coefficient vector and subscript n indicates the mode shape number, [6]. 
According to the FEMA 356, 90 % of the total mass participation must be included in the response 
spectrum analysis, to determine if the higher modes are significant for the structural response. A 
response spectrum analysis shall be performed for only the first mode shape. The higher modes are 
considered significant if the shear response in any stores of the 90 % mass participation exceeds 130 
% of the storey shear for the first mode, [10].  
 
2.5 Soil-Structure Interaction 
The dynamic response of a structure subjected to strong ground motions from an earthquake is 
affected by the characteristic of the soil layers at the foundation and the soil surrounding the structure. 
This phenomenon is a soil-structure interaction (SsI), and it is a well-known the relationship between 
the vibration periods of the structure and the surrounding soil. When an earthquake occurs, the 
response of the structure depends on several factors, and the most important once are (a) the physical 
structural properties, (b) the characteristics of the strong ground motions and (c) the soil surrounding 
the structure. In a case of an earthquake, the coupling of the soil and structure will change the 
foundation motion compared to a free-field case; this is the soil-structure interaction. The soil 
structure interaction is a result of; (a) the structural produced vibration transfers/radiate energy from 
the structure back to the soil, and (b) present structure’s impact in the wave scattering. The importance 
of the SsI depends on the soil conditions, and the fundamental period of the site and the fundamental 
period of the structure, [11]. The depth of the soil stratum 𝐻 and the shear wave velocity of the soil 
𝑉𝑠 defines the fundament period of the site. 
 
 
𝑇𝑠= 4𝐻
𝑉𝑠
 
 
(2.32) 
If the fundamental period of the site is longer than the fundamental period of the structure, accounting 
for soil-structure interaction in the FE model is prudent. In the case, the fundamental period of the 
==PAGE:32==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
14 
 
site is less than the fundamental period of the structure; the SsI will reduce the response of the 
structure, [11]. 
 
The depth of the soil stratum might not always be a known factor, but the importance of SsI can still 
be determined. For firm soils, a realistic result is obtained by assuming fixed base structure and 
assuming the foundation follows the incident waves with minimal or no SsI. The SsI effect is 
significant in the loose and soft soil, due to the soil structure increase in flexibility and thereby 
increase the fundamental period of the structure. The radiation damping can also affect the SsI, 
radiation damping occurs when a structural foundation, embedded in the soil, are encountered by 
seismic waves, and the waves are reflected back into the soil from the foundation. This phenome is 
called waves scattering, and it increases the damping of the soil,  [12].
 
 
==PAGE:33==
Operational Modal Analysis 
 
15 
 
 
Over the past few decades, Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) have become increasingly popular, 
as an experimental method to estimate the dynamic parameter of a structure. The cornerstone in OMA 
is, the structural responses are solely from the obtained ambient measurements, and when performing 
the measurements, the structures are exposed to natural loads giving in its natural state. A robust 
quality with OMA is the loads on the structure does not need to be determined or estimated, but as a 
critical fundamental assumption, the input forces follows a Gaussian probability density function. 
The Gaussian assumption is valid via the central limit theorem for a large variety of natural excitation 
forces, such as traffic loads on bridges, wind loads on structures, when a sufficient amount of data 
samples are obtained. 
The fundamental idea behind OMA and analysing the random vibrations response of structures is, 
when a structure is excited by a random force, the structural response is not entirely random anymore 
due to the structural response will tend to vibrate at its natural frequencies. Performing OMA, there 
are certain assumptions which need to be satisfied, before the OMA is valid, [13].   
– Forces are assumed to be Gaussian distributed 
– The response is assumed to be stationary and ergodic 
– The system is assumed linear, with low damping ratio – under 10-15% 
– Mode shapes are geometrically orthogonal 
 
The definition of stationary for a random process yields the statistical properties are independent of 
time, meaning the mean value of a process a time point 𝑡1is qual to the mean value a random process 
at time 𝑡2. A random process is ergodic if it’s stationary and the time properties and the realization 
properties are equal, and further the mean value of one realization is equal to the stationary mean 
value. Generally if the density function of a random signal 𝑥(𝑡) is time independent and all 
expectations are time independent too, then the signal is considered stationary,  [14]. 
 
3.1 Time and Frequency Domain 
In OMA various algorithms exist to estimate the response of a structure. Two main subcategories of 
calculations methods exist the frequency domain methods and the time domain. Signals in the time 
domain can be complicated, but with a Fourier transformation of the signals becomes simple in the 
frequency domain.  
==PAGE:34==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance  
 
16 
 
This section is dedicated to the theoretical understanding of signal correlation, spectral density and 
dynamic estimation in the time and frequency domain. 
 
3.1.1 Signal Correlation 
The correlation analysis of two signals is a typical study for signal processing. A correlation analysis 
of two different signals describes the cross-correlation of the signals, which says how well the two 
signals fit with each other. Autocorrelation describes how a signal is correlated with itself. Two 
signals or points at one signal are considered perfectly correlated when the signals have identical and 
constant amplitude. The opposite of perfect correlation is zero correlation, which theoretically two 
different random signals evidently will be. The correlations functions are essential for OMA since it 
is an essential element for estimating the dynamics modal parameters (frequencies, damping ratio and 
mode shapes) in the case the signals are Gaussian distributed.   
 
3.1.2 Autocorrelation 
Having a single time varying signal as illustrated in Figure 4, the autocorrelation of the signal 
describes how adjacent the points are correlated.  The autocorrelation of a stochastic and ergodic time 
signal 𝑥(𝑡) with time lag 𝜏, is the mean value of 𝑥(𝑡)𝑥(𝑡+ 𝜏). The autocorrelation function 𝑅𝑥(𝜏) is 
symmetry and reach its maximal value equal to the variance 𝜎𝑥
2 at perfect correlation condition with 
zero time lag, and a zero value of the autocorrelation function when the time lag is high, [14]. For 
harmonic functions when the time lag reach half of the period of the signal itself, the correlation is, 
considered perfect with opposite sign, and the correlation function is a harmonic itself. The harmonic 
signal is considered perfect correlated when 𝜏= 0, [15].  
 
Figure 4, Autocorrelation of two points along a time-varying signal, from [15].

Operational Modal Analysis

Defining the autocorrelation function as 
 
 
𝑅𝑥(𝜏) = E[𝑥(𝑡)𝑥(𝑡+ 𝜏)]  
 
(3.1) 
The assumption a signal is stationary lead to a non-time varying density function, which leads to the 
correlation function is time independent, and the results do not change when changing the time t. A 
time shift – 𝜏 leads to the symmetry relation 
 
 
𝑅𝑥(𝜏) = E[𝑥(𝑡−𝜏)𝑥(𝑡)] = 𝑅𝑥(−𝜏) 
 
(3.2) 
 
3.1.3 Cross-correlation               
Conducting multiple measurements with different sensors and setups of a structure the cross-
correlation function is a useful tool to estimate the correlation of two-time signals. The cross-
correlation of two stationary time varying signals 𝑥(𝑡) and 𝑦(𝑡). 
 
 
𝑅𝑥𝑦(𝜏) = E[𝑥(𝑡)𝑦(𝑡+ 𝜏)]  
𝑅𝑦𝑥(𝜏) = E[𝑦(𝑡)𝑥(𝑡+ 𝜏)]  
 
(3.3) 
The definition of the cross-correlation is the mean of the cross product of the signals, from which the 
dependence between the two different signals is revealed, by assuming zero mean signals. If the cross 
product has zero mean, the signals are uncorrelated, and if the signals are correlated the cross product 
means is either positive or negative. The symmetry relation for the cross-correlation obtained by the 
time shift – 𝜏 and assuming the signal are stationary, analog to the autocorrelation, [14].  
 
3.1.4 Correlation Functions 
Conducting vibration measurements of structures a set of M recorded measurement channels, the 
response vector arrange the measurements channels, which includes the response of N measurement 
channels, and not only a single measurement. 
  
 
𝒚(𝑡) = {𝑦1(𝑡), 𝑦2(𝑡), … , 𝑦𝑀(𝑡)}𝑇 
 
(3.4) 
From the response vector, the correlation function (CF) is defined as a matrix including both 
autocorrelation and cross-correlation responses. The diagonal element is autocorrelated, and the off-
diagonal element is cross-correlated functions. The formulation of CF is similar to Equation (3.2).

The DFT analysis of a signal is relatively computational substantial because the transformation of the 
complete signal is in one sequence. The Fast Fourier Transform (FFT) algorithm is applied to 
minimise the computational effort; the algorithm builds on the DFT. The FFT algorithm splits up the 
signal 𝑦𝑗 in shorter sequences and calculate the DFT for the specific sequence. By combining the 
sequences, the FFT algorithm calculates the total DFT response, and the truncation error is minimised. 
A drawback of the DFT is the selection of N affect how precise the estimation of the actual frequency 
of the signal, and the energy distribution in the frequency spectrum. An advanced with DFT is it 
stratifies Equation (3.15) at each signal sequence, since the DFT is not a method for an infinite signal 
but a finite signal.

3.2 Identification Algorithms in The Time and Frequency Domain 
The identification algorithms are divided into three categories, either the time domain, frequency 
domain or a combination of time and frequency domain. Estimating the dynamic parameters by the 
time domain approach, calculation the auto and cross-correlation of the signal, and dynamic 
parameters are estimated from the decay of the correlation functions. The dynamics parameters are 
estimated using the power spectral density function (PSD) in the frequency domain methods, and as 
a fast method to estimate the modes in the frequency domain is by using the simple “peak-picking” 
==PAGE:40==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance  
 
22 
 
method at the PSD plot. A significant peak in the PSD graph is associated with a modes shape of the 
structure.  The combined time and frequency domain identification algorithm is beneficial to enhance 
the estimation of the structural response. Combining the Frequency Domain Decomposition (FDD) 
with Ibrahim Time Domain (ITD) enhances the estimation of structural damping, due to ITD is a 
robust estimator of damping, and the estimation of damping contain a high uncertainty, different 
identification algorithms will estimate different damping values.   
 
3.2.1 Time Domain 
In the time domain, the correlation function is essential for estimating the dynamic structural 
parameters, because the correlation functions obtain physical information about the structure. The 
correlation function will follow the structural response. Consider a structure without any damping, 
when a force excites the structure, the response of the structure is vibrations at its natural frequency, 
and the response is harmonically. The correlation function obtains the physical information about the 
response, and the correlation function for the harmonically response is a harmonic function with equal 
frequency. Consider a structure with damping, the response of a random force will decay with time. 
Thus the correlation function will decay time, and the decay rate estimates the damping ratio. 
 
Figure 5 illustrates the four general steps in identification of the dynamic parameters of a structure 
using the OMA time domain approach. a) The structural response 𝑦(𝑡) of a given measurement, this 
is often accelerations. b) Using Equation (3.2) and (3.3) to calculate the auto- and cross-correlation 
functions of the obtained measurements. Applying window filtering of the tail of the correlation 
function is cut off, and by applying an appropriate window filtering the modal parameters are not 
significant changed for the filtered signal, contrary the added noise from the filtering. c) The 
correlation functions are utilized as an input for the OMA performance, and the algorithms in the time 
domain will try to fit the correlation function with a fitting technique. The time domain algorithm 
Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI) divagate from the above statement, the correlation function 
is calculated but not treated directly. This is illustrated in section 3.2.2 - Stochastic Subspace 
Identification. d) The output of OMA techniques contains the Eigenvalues 𝜆𝑖 from which the modal 
parameters 𝑓𝑛 and 𝜁𝑛 and the Eigenvectors (mode shapes) 𝜙 are estimated. From the estimated modal 
parameters the correlation function 𝑅̂(𝜏) is calculated, and by comparing with the correlation function 
𝑅(𝑡) calculated from the response signal 𝑦(𝑡), it is possible to address the quality of the estimation.  
==PAGE:41==
Operational Modal Analysis 
 
23 
 
The estimation quality of the modal parameters depends on the amount of noise contained in 
correlation function, and the quality of the fitting technique. In practice, the noise will always be 
present in the correlation function. 
 
 Figure 5, The four general steps in time domain identification, a) the response of the system, b) calculation of the 
correlation function, c) performing OMA with the correlation function as input and d) estimating of the modal parameters, 
illustration from [13]. 
3.2.2 Stochastic Subspace Identification 
The Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI) technique is widely used in commercial programs such 
as ARTeMIS. The SSI technique has two primary methods the Data-Driven Stochastic Subspace 
Identification (DD-SSI) and the Covariance-Driven Stochastic Subspace Identification (Cov-SSI), 
the latter identification method is similar to the Eigensystem Realization Algorithm and for further 
explanation of the method the author referrers to the literature, for instance, Brincker and Ventura 
[15]. This section will explain the Data-Driven Stochastic Subspace Identification that is the methods 
implemented in ARTeMIS.  
Considering the stochastic response of a system in time with 𝑀 measurements, the response vector 
becomes  
 
 
𝒚(𝑡) = {𝑦1(𝑡), 𝑦2(𝑡), … , 𝑦𝑀(𝑡)}𝑇 
 
(3.23) 
==PAGE:42==

24 
 
Assuming the number of measurements is higher than one, the system is a multi-degree of freedom 
system with the classical equation of motion

(3.24) 
As for the classical definition 𝐌, 𝐂 and 𝐊 is the mass, damping and stiffness matrices of the system 
respectively, and 𝐟 is the force vector.

3.2.2.4 The Kalman States 
The initial conditions for the projection matrix are the Kalman states, and the projection matrix is 
express as 
 
 
𝐎= 𝚪𝑠𝐗0 
 
(3.38) 
𝐗0 is the Kalman states at zero time lag, but the observability matrix is still know. In order to estimate 
the initial condition and observability matrix QR decomposition is performed of the transposed block 
Hankel matrix giving and upper triangular matrix 𝐑 and an orthogonal projection matrix 𝐎. 
Performing Singular Value Decomposition (SVD) the projection matrix is  
 
 
𝐎= 𝐔𝐒𝐕𝑇 
 
(3.39)

and 𝐔 of 𝑛/2 modes meaning the matrices are reduces to the singular values. The initial condition 
and observability matrix are now express as

3.2.3 Frequency Domain 
In the frequency domain, the spectral densities are essential for estimating the dynamic structural 
parameters, because of the spectral densities, which is related to the correlation function, obtain 
physical information about the structure. Both advantages and disadvantages are related to the 
frequency domain, two of the main advantages at the frequency domain are: 
1) The dynamic estimation of the modal parameter is simplified because each mode is said to 
dominate a small frequency band, where the mode can be estimated. 
2) The method is in general considerably more straightforward than other estimation methods. 
 
The main disadvantages are the estimation is not bias-free, because each mode not only contributed 
to its dominating resonance peak, but it also contributes to the whole frequency band.  
Instrumentation of vibration measurement is often in the time domain, and Fourier transformation is 
applied to transform the measurement into the frequency domain by using the DFT Equation (3.16). 
As aforementioned structural system tends to vibrate at its natural frequency, and if the system 
response is measured, the system frequency is often illustrated as the magnitude of Fourier transform 
in the frequency domain. It is relatively often the case the system response is not measured, but a 
response from several measurement points with multiple sensors. To estimate the modal parameters 
for such measurements the cross-spectral density giving in Equation (3.19) is applied to account for 
the phase shift between different measurement channels.  
  
Figure 6 illustrates the four general steps in identification of the dynamic parameters of a structure 
using the OMA frequency domain approach. a) Identical to step a) in Figure 5, the structural response 
𝑦(𝑡) of a given measurement. b) Calculation of the spectral densities using Equation (3.19) and the 
corresponding cross-spectral power-density from Equation (3.20). By plotting the spectral densities 
the width and frequency peak for the modes are estimated. c) The spectral density is utilized as an 
input for the OMA performance, and the techniques in the frequency domain will try to fit the spectral 
densities to estimate the Eigenvalues and -vectors of the system. d) The output of OMA techniques 
contains the Eigenvalues 𝜆𝑖 which are transform into the modal parameters 𝑓𝑛 and 𝜁𝑛 and the 
Eigenvectors 𝜙 are estimated. The quality of the estimation is studied by comparing the estimated 
==PAGE:48==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance  
 
30 
 
modal parameters from the spectral density 𝐺̂x(𝑓), with the spectral density 𝐺𝑥(𝑓) calculated from 
the response signal 𝑦(𝑡), [13]. 
 
Figure 6, The four general steps in frequency domain identification, a) the response of the system, b) calculation of the 
spectral densities, c) performing OMA and estimating the Eigenvalues and -vectors from the spectral densities and d) 
estimating of the modal parameters, illustration from [13]. 
For more detailed explanations of the Classical Frequency-Domain Decomposition and the 
Frequency-Domain Decomposition methods, the author refers to Brincker and Ventura [15], and 
other literature.  
 
3.3 Modal Assurance Criterion  
In OMA multiple tests are often performed on the same structure, and it is beneficial to compare the 
results obtained from the different tests or different identification algorithms/techniques. For a single 
degree of freedom system, comparing the natural frequency and the damping is a straightforward 
procedure, but when we deal with multiple degrees of freedom systems, the mode shape vectors are 
not a straightforward procedure to compare. The Modal Assurance Criterion (MAC) is a function, 
which provides a measure of consistency/degree of linearity between two experimental or analytical 
mode shapes, [15]. The MAC defined value of consistency is between zero and the unity of one since 
==PAGE:49==
Operational Modal Analysis 
 
31 
 
the MAC value is normalised by the magnitude of the vector. A MAC value close to one does not 
necessarily mean the mode shapes are correct, only there is consistency.  
 
 
MAC(a, b) =
|{𝜙𝑎}𝑇{𝜙𝑏}|2
({𝜙𝑎}𝑇{𝜙𝑎})({𝜙𝑏}𝑇{𝜙𝑏}) 
(3.49) 
   
Where 𝜙𝑎and 𝜙𝑏 are mode shape vectors respectably. The two terms in the denomination are the 
squared lengths of the vectors. By realizing the MAC is a real-value scalar, the equation can be 
rewriting in means of the Hermitian matrix, which is the complex conjugated of a matrix/vector, [21]. 
 
AC(a, b) =
|{𝜙𝑎}𝐻{𝜙𝑏}|2
({𝜙𝑎}𝐻{𝜙𝑎})({𝜙𝑏}𝐻{𝜙𝑏}) 
(3.50) 
 
 
3.4 Ambient Vibration Test 
Ambient Vibration Test (AVT) is an experimental non-destructive technique to determine the 
dynamic properties of a structure, including mode shapes, frequencies and damping. Other 
applications of AVT include structural health monitoring; detecting changes in the structural 
response/natural frequency due to structural damage, calibration of numerical models of the structure, 
this is an essential aspect of the AVT. Applications for AVT includes bridges, dams and multi-storey 
buildings, [22]. The AVT technique relies on the excitation of the structure is ambient produced by 
the traffic, humans or wind, which is a keystone for the operational modal analysis. There are several 
benefits with AVT. First, it is a full-scale experimental test which captures the real structural response 
to the given ambient excitation; it is economical compared with forced-vibration test there is no need 
for heavy loads. Multiple vibration modes are excited, and single measurement devices are able 
detected theses vibrations modes. The AVT does not interfere with the use of the structures; there is 
no need to close down the structure for a more extended period, for bridges the traffic is undisturbed. 
The AVT does not introduce damage to the structure, which could occur with a classic forced mass 
vibration test. The main disadvantages with AVT are the amount of measurement data; the 
measurements are often around 20-30 minutes in duration depending on the period of the structure, 
and uncertainty about the estimation of the structural damping.  
 
==PAGE:50==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance  
 
32 
 
According to the American National Standard ANSI 2.47 [23], the length of a random signal to 
minimise the errors as variance and bias problems is a function of the fundamental period in Hz and 
the damping ratio. Ensuring a variation error of 10% and a bias error of 4% duration of the 
measurements should be 
 
 
𝑇= 200
𝜁𝑓
 
 
(3.51) 
Brincker and Ventura [15], address the issues with having the above requirement. In practice, this 
means OMA measurement recordings will be serval hours long, for tall buildings with low 
frequencies, and would not be cost-efficient. Another way to estimate the sufficient measurement 
duration is by looking at the maximum correlation time for the response.  Allowing at least 20 data 
segments to estimate a reasonable correlation function of the response, leads to a requirement for the 
total length of the time series at, [15]. 
 
 
 
𝑇𝑡𝑜𝑡> 20
2𝜁𝑓=
10
𝜁𝑓𝑚𝑖𝑛
 
 
(3.52) 
This requirement is significantly more cost-effective for the Operational Modal Analysis in practice 
since the duration length is reduced from hours to around 15 min – 25 min for tall buildings and 
similar structures with low natural frequencies. 
 
3.4.1 Prerequisites for the Ambient Vibration Analysis 
A random process in combination with a deterministic signal characterises ambient vibration. The 
random process attributes the vibration mode response of the structure, and the deterministic signal 
attributes the randomness of the white noise and environmental excitation.  
 
==PAGE:51==
Structural Description 
 
33 
 
 
The building investigated in this study is the City Crest Tower located at 1155 Homer Street in 
Downtown Vancouver in British Columbia Canada. The building is a podium tower structure, a high-
rise building with a two-storey townhouse linked together with two walkway bridges at the east side 
of the tower. The second floor is extended around 10 meters as the terrace, and the terrace linked to 
the tower with a monolithically floor slab. The underground structure consists of a parking garage 
with six half elevations levels. The podium tower is a residential tower, and the shape is representative 
of the residential structures in Vancouver. The townhouse is not considered further in this study. 
Figure 7, illustrates the overall dimensions of the tower, the height of the tower is 83.2 m with 32 
storeys above the ground level, and a storey height of 2.6 m for all the floors except the ground floor, 
which is 4 m. The podium tower has six levels of parking below the ground; the dimensions are 62.3 
m in the East-West direction and 35.8 m in the North-South direction, the 6th parking level is located 
9.4 m below the ground surface. The overall tower dimensions of the floor layout measure 24.7 m in 
East-West and 22 m in North-South, with an extension of 9.8 m in the North-South direction for the 
second level. The floor plan is reduced thought out the height of the tower, from 600 m2 for floor 
three through 10, 500 m2 for floor 11 through 25, and from floor 25 to 32 the floor areas are reduced 
further. The width of the concrete core is 7.6 m North-South direction and 10.3 m the East-West 
direction. The layout for each floor plan given in Appendix B - AutoCAD Drawings. 
 
Figure 7, Typical floor layout of the City Crest Tower, from Schuster [24] with permission.

5.1 Structural System 
The structure was designed according to Vancouver Buildings By-law 6134, CAN/CSA-S413-87 for 
the parking structure, and the Canadian reinforced concrete design standard CSA CAN3-A23.3-M84. 
The structure was constructed from autumn 1992 to January 1994. 
The tower consists of reinforced concrete, and the main Seismic Force Resisting System (SFRS) is 
the concrete core, located in the centre of the floor. Columns are placed along the edge of each floor 
to support the floor slap, and the columns are a part of the load-bearing gravity system. The high-rise 
==PAGE:53==

35 
 
and the townhouse are considered fully connected from parking level P6 to the ground level with 
structural elements as slabs, columns and shear walls. At the second level, two walkways with 
expansions joint connect the tower and the townhouse. At level 30 and above the columns at the 
perimeter are replaced with thin walls.

(illustrated as grey elements at the figure). The red colour indicates the floor slab, and the concrete

229 mm.  For the parking levels, four different types of slabbands are applied, with a thickness of 457

Figure 7 and Figure 8 shows multiple irregularities of the structure, and the tower is an asymmetric

geometry from the parking levels to the tower levels. Form a stiff squared concrete box structure with 
concrete walls in the perimeter and serval shear walls to a concrete tower with perimeter columns and 
one concrete core.

5.1.6 Foundation 
The foundation is a line and mat foundation, the applied line foundation to each wall element, and 
the mat foundation supports the columns. The foundation of the concrete core is one square 
foundation mat. Figure 12 shows the foundation plan from the structural drawings. The dimension of 
the columns mat foundation differs from column to column, with the smallest foundation mat 
measuring 2.13 m by 2.44 m in the top areal and a thickness of 0.66 m. The larges measuring 4.12 m 
by 4.12 m in the top real and a thickness of 1.17 m. The squared concrete core mat foundation 
measures 12.80 m by 16.15 m with a thickness of 1.17 m. The width of the shear wall foundation 
varies from 0.61 m to 1.83 m and with a thickness varying from 0.25 m to 0.51 m.  
 
The soil conditions for the location in Downtown Vancouver for the foundation is “firm ground” Soil 
Class C in NBCC 2015 with an average Vs30 shear wave velocity at 450 m/s, [26]. The soil-structure 
interaction is limited in this case because of the firm soil and, the soil-structure interaction from the 
incident waves from an earthquake source can be assumed to have minimal effect on these types of 
structures.

Ambient Vibration Test Results

This section will explain the experimental Ambient Vibration Test (AVT) setup of the City Crest 
Tower, and present the Operational Modal Analysis results with different estimation techniques. 
 
6.1 Instrumentation Devices 
The Ambient Vibration Test of the City Crest Tower was performed in the summer of 1992 with the 
Hybrid Bridge Evaluation System. The system is a data acquisition and analysis system developed by 
PhD Felber, at UBC. The application of the system was to acquire the ambient vibrations and 
identification of bridge systems. Norman D. Schuster, a UBC PhD graduate, further developed the 
acquisition system to accommodate AVT of buildings. The ambient vibration measurements used for 
this project was acquired with the data acquisition program AVDA updated version of AVTEST. 
The Ambient Vibration Test was performed with eight Kinematics forced balanced sensors - the 
FBA-11 accelerometer. Cables (lengths of 90 m – 300 m) connecting the FBA sensors with the signal 
conditioner. The signal conditioner filter and amplified the raw FBA signals, and the signals were 
monitored and converted from analogue to digital to store the data as binary files. Figure 13 illustrates 
a flowchart of the data processing on site from FBA raw measurement to the stored binary files.   
 
Figure 13, Data acquisition equipment, from [24] with permission from the author. 
==PAGE:60==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
42 
 
6.1.1 Test Objectives And Sensor Location 
The primary objective of the AVT was to determine the dynamic characteristic of a high-rise building. 
Norman D. Schuster performed multiple tests to investigate the development of the dynamic 
characteristics during construction of the high-rise building. For the dynamic analysis of the structure 
mode shapes and modal periods/frequencies were of interest. Mode shapes are of interest to 
understand the deformation of the structure. Considering time-varying loads the modal 
periods/frequencies are necessary to indicate which dominant structural frequencies will excite the 
structure. The original AVT had a second objective, to assess the foundation rocking of the structure, 
by placing accelerometer in the basement measuring vertical and lateral accelerations. The AVT 
performed by Norman D. Schuster encompass six different AVTs at different construction time. 
 
Sensor location is a crucial parameter of an AVT to capture the dynamic characteristic of the building. 
The applied AVT focused on the global response of the structure and a one-floor vibration test on the 
second floor. The sensors were mounted to the floor using anchor bolts to ensure the same location 
of sensors during the different tests. Eight sensors were used to the AVT, and four were roving sensors 
(moving from setup to setup) and four reference sensors. The sensors were located according to 
guidelines from the California Strong Motion Instrumentation Program, to ensure adequate isolation 
of translation and torsional motion in both North-South and East-West direction for each floor. The 
reference sensors must be located at antinodes of the vibration modes of interest and were located on 
the 29th floor (in the top of the building). Figure 14 illustrates a typical roving sensor layout for a 
floor. Figure 15 illustrates two FBA sensors mounted to the floor during one test setup. 
 
 
==PAGE:61==

Figure 15, FBA-11 sensor mounted on the floor, from [24] with permission from the author.

and Figure 20.

This study focus on the Ambient Vibration Test performed during the week of June 5th – 6th  1993, 
during this stage of the construction only the first lower floors had installed partition walls, and the 
roof was cast two weeks prior the test. The test was assumed a reinforced concrete structure solely, 
without partitions walls, which impacts the global damping and add addition floor loads. Only the 
global modal frequencies and mode shapes were of interested, which reflects the sampling rate of 40 
Hz, giving a Nyquist frequency of 𝑓𝑐 20 Hz. The ambient vibration measurements were filtered onsite 
with a low pass filter of 0.1 Hz and a high pass filter of 12.5 Hz. Determined frequencies higher than 
12.5 Hz will still be possible, but the effect of the filter will reduce the magnitude of the mode. 
Table 4 illustrates the field notes from the ambient vibration tests, where some channels had drifts 
and shorts issues. The crane test setup is out of scope for this study. The duration of each measurement 
is 13 minutes and 39.2 seconds, corresponding to 32768 data points.

6.2 Numerical ARTeMIS Model 
The Operational Modal Analysis of the ambient test result was analysed in ARTeMIS Modal Pro 
version 4, to obtain frequencies and mode shapes of the global (entire) structure based on the 18 
different test setups. Additionary to the model of the global structure and local model of the second 
floor was implemented, to capture the mode shapes and frequencies of the specific floor. Both 
ARTeMIS models replicate the sensor position as detailed as possible since it is desired to compare 
the mode shapes from the ARTeMIS models with the Finite Element Models. The geometry does not 
affect or bias the frequencies obtained from ARTeMIS if the sensor direction is correct.  
The computed ARTeMIS models from input configuration files, which carries information about the 
orientation of nodes, surface areas, linked nodes, for the test setups the files see Appendix D - 
ARTeMIS Configuration files. 
 
6.2.1 Geometry of The ARTeMIS Models 
The sensor locations and not the outer geometry of the building is the basis of the geometry of the 
models. The modelled geometry in ARTeMIS is for a general floor 21.9 m by 19.5 m, which is smaller 
==PAGE:66==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
48 
 
than the real structure, which is 28.3 m by 24.7 m. When modelling after the sensors positions, the 
known response is at this exact position from the ambient response of the structure. At all other 
positions, the repose will be an estimation, and therefore it is not desired to compute a complex model 
in ARTeMIS since we are only interested in the sensor mode shape response of the sensors.  
Modelling surface area as floor slabs in ARTeMIS requires dividing the surface area into multiple 
triangles and defining each node with coordinates in x, y and z-direction. ARTeMIS requires for each 
assigned measurement data channel a specific node, and the direction of the measurements in x, y 
and z-direction. For all the models of the global structure and the second level, the location (0, 0) 
corresponds to the sensor located in the Northwest corner of the structure. The global structures 
included four different computed floor models. The general floor plan has six nodes, three sensors 
and fours surface areas, illustrated at Figure 21 and Figure 22. The models for the Parking level P6 
and the second level have 10-12 nodes introducing 12-13 surface areas for only one floor. The 
illustrated reference points in Figure 22 corresponds to the location of sensor number 1 at the other 
floors.

6.2.2 Signal Processing – Global Structure 
The performed signal processing of the AVT data was in ARTeMIS Pro modal version 4.0. Figure 
25 illustrates the singular values of spectral densities for all the measurements setups. For spectral 
density estimation in the ARTeMIS, for the estimation, no additional filters were applied, and the 
==PAGE:69==
Ambient Vibration Test Results 
 
51 
 
applied resolution of the Spectral Density Estimation/segment size was 1024. The figure illustrates 
the harmonics of the structures form 0-10 Hz, in this area of the spectrum, the harmonica has the 
characteristic shape of a tapered bell shape. The frequency range of the ambient vibration test and the 
spectral density estimation was 0-20 Hz.  
 
Figure 25, Singular values of spectral densities for all test setups.  
The applied estimation methods were Enhanced Frequency-Domain Decomposition (EFDD) and 
Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI) for estimation of mode shapes and the corresponding modal 
damping. The applied two different estimation methods enhancing the confidence in the estimated 
mode shapes, modal frequencies and modal damping. The applied SSI method in ARTeMIS is a data-
driven method where the determination of the weight matrix is by the unweighted principal 
component (UPC) algorithm. The ambient vibration test included multiple test setups; the applied 
SSI algorithm was the UPC Merged algorithm. This algorithm is dedicated to analyses’ with multiple 
test setups, [27]. The SSI - UPC  Merged analysis in ARTeMIS indicated noise modes incorrectly for 
multiple real modes, the first modes in North-South and East-West, the author decided not to use the 
SSI - UPC Merged method to estimate the response, but applying the SSI – UPC method.  
The ARTeMIS analysis estimated for both the EFFD and the SSI - UPC method 12 modes shapes 
with corresponding modal frequencies and modal damping. Figure 26 illustrates the singular values 
of the spectral densities from the EFFD analysis in ARTeMIS. From the analysis, the shown modal 
frequencies are as bell peaks at the plot. The first 12 mode shapes from 0-10 Hz are “picked”, the 
==PAGE:70==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
52 
 
green band at the figure indicates the modal domain. The first two mode shapes were closely spaced, 
and they correspond to the first longitudinal mode shape in North-South and East-West direction, the 
modal frequencies listed in Table 5. From Table 5 the difference in modal frequency for the first two 
modes was 0.1 Hz. 
 
Figure 26, Singular values of spectral densities from the EFFD analysis. 
The EFFD method estimates the modal damping for each corresponding modal frequency. Evaluating 
the results from Table 5 the first mode in North-South had a damping ratio of 3.5%, first mode in 
East-West 2.09 % and the first torsional had 1.04% the rest of the modes listed in Table 5 variated in 
damping ratio from 0.8 to 1.7 %. According to the literature Chopra Table 11.2.1 [6], the damping 
values for a reinforced concrete building with working stress no more than ½ of the yield point is in 
the range of 3-5 %. During the AVT, every structural element was behaving linear-elastic, with that 
in mind the damping values were corresponding to expected values for a reinforced concrete building. 
Most building codes assume a damping ratio of 5 %, for the structure with partitions walls and all 
==PAGE:71==
Ambient Vibration Test Results 
 
53 
 
installations. The performed AVT was during construction where the number of installed partitions 
walls were limited, and therefore the estimated damping ratios were concording with the 
recommended values and the building code assumption.

Table 7 list the result of the performed comparison of the results from the EFDD and the SSI – UPC 
methods. As expected the frequency differences were relatively low, the most significant difference 
was 4 % between the two estimators. The estimation of the damping values for each mode was entirely 
different, and this was expected to see this difference since the damping value estimation contains a 
high uncertainty. As mentioned in the theory section about the EFDD method, the method has 
difficulties estimating the damping, and combining the algorithm with the Ibrahim Time Domain is 
beneficial for damping estimations. The estimated damping value was within a similar damping range 
for both estimations methods, 0.8 to 3.5 % for the EFDD method and 0.9 to 3.4 % for the SSI – UPC 
method. For this point and on, then referring to the results of the AVT, it is the results from the SSI 
– UPC method.

As aforementioned, the measurement duration of the AVT is essential, and the minimum requirement 
to perform Operational Modal Analysis and be confident the length of the duration is significant was 
calculated with the giving formula from Brincker and Ventura, [15]. The duration criterion was 
estimated with a natural frequency of 0.57 Hz and a damping ratio of 3.88 %, as illustrated in Equation 
(6.1) the total duration of the measurement should be longer than 7 minutes and 32 seconds. The total 
duration of the measurement was 13 minutes and 39.2 seconds, and thus measurement duration was 
sufficient.

biased of any torsional part. For the fourth mode shape, the longitudinal mode shapes start to show

==PAGE:81==
Linear Finite Element Model Analysis 
 
63 
 
 
Linear elastic Finite Element Model of the podium tower built from the structural paper drawings in 
the commercial program SAP 2000 version 19 from Computers and Structures Inc. The model 
approach was to model the podium tower with a high level of detailing and following the structural 
drawings as closely as possible. The geometry of slabs, walls and columns of the podium tower were 
the first modelled in AutoCAD from the structural drawings and then imported to SAP 2000.  
 
7.1 SAP 2000 modelling  
The model assumptions for the linear elastic model follows the concrete is considered to fully 
uncracked since the performed ambient vibration test was during construction and the linear elastic 
conditions the FE-Model has to reflect the same conditions, furthermore no recorded earthquakes of 
influence during construction. The linear model lead to the steel reinforcement were not physically 
modelled as rebar in the concrete elements, but the mass density of the concrete at increased, see 7.1.1 
Materials. The two-storey townhouse was not model since it was assumed not to have a significant 
effect on the structural response of the podium tower. Furthermore at the ground level at the South 
and West side of the tower steel overhangs were neglected, and at the roof, the steel overhangs were 
neglected too, since the influence of the global response were assumed to be minimal. 
 
In SAP 2000, it is possible to adjust the connections flexibility between structural elements, when the 
centre-to-centre connectivity is applied; the rigid zone of the connection is a range between zero and 
one. SAP 2000 recommends for reinforced concrete structures the connection flexibility of 0.5, which 
is the applied value for this model. SAP 2000 defines a rigid zone factor with the value of zero, as a 
connection with a non-rigid zone and the structural elements connected at the centre points. SAP 2000 
defines a rigid zone factor of one, as a connection where a rigid plate connects the elements, and thus 
each of the elements is shorter with the dimensions of the rigid zone, [28].  
 
The connectivity and offset between the columns, walls, beams and floors slabs are of high 
importance, and each structural element as columns, wall and beams are offset with the exact 
dimension to simulate the real intersections points. Meaning the length of the columns starts above 
the floor slab and end under the slab for the level above. Of more importance is the columns are offset 
in the x and y-direction for the global coordinate system, to place the column at it right position 
==PAGE:82==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
64 
 
according to the structural drawings. SAP 2000 will as default place the column at its geometric 
centre, and if this is not true, an offset of the structural element is necessary. See Appendix E - Linear 
Analysis Results for the offset of each column and beam. When creating the model the sign 
conventions are critical since the offset is in the local axis of the structural element. For the created 
models all the structural elements were drawn from the bottom to the top or from the right (East) to 
the left (West). All the structural elements were checked to follow this sign convention. 
 
During the modelling phase, an inconsistency observation from the structural drawings: Elevation 1 
for the South core wall at the structural drawing S19: South core wall elevation – only one door 
illustrated from level 10 - 24. The floor plans say two doors at structural drawing page S11. Decision 
door at southeast corner is a mistake at floor plan S11; there are not two doors to one unit on the 
architectural drawings. 
 
7.1.1 Materials 
The implemented linear model in SAP2000 did not include the specific steel reinforcement in each 
concrete member. To account for the additional reinforcement an increasing the density mass of the 
concrete was applied, to accounts for the additional mass from the steel reinforcement in the concrete. 
Pure concrete has an average weight of 2400 kg/m3 (24kN/ m3) and accounting for the weight of steel 
reinforcement the applied concrete mass density was 2500 kg/m3 (25kN/ m3), [29]. 
 
The structural concrete materials properties for the FE-Model model are defined in section 5 - 
Structural Description. Table 8 list the applied concrete Young E-modulus, according to Eurocode 2 
1992-1-1, [30].

7.1.2 Structural Elements 
Modelling shell elements in SAP2000 the software provides two different shell assumptions in the 
linear range, with the shell, is considered thick or thin. SAP2000 stats the main difference between 
the two models are the shear deformation in the plate-bending behaviour. The thick plate accounts 
for the transverse shear deformation and follows the Mindlin/Reissner formulation, where the thin 
plate neglects the transverse shear deformation and follows the Kirchhoff formulation. The thick shell 
elements are sensitive to the meshing of the element, and a too coarse meshing will lead to an 
overestimate the shell stiffness. In the pure-bending deformation case, the thin shells are slightly more 
accurate than the thick shells, if the shear deformation is neglectable, this is because the thin shells 
are more flexible than the thick. If the shell elements have geometry discontinuities as openings or 
adjacent openings where transverse shear deformation occurs, and both stresses and deformations are 
of concern, thick shells elements are applied to account for the transverse shear deformation. 
SAP2000 recommends using the thick shell with a refined mesh, [31]. The shells elements are 
modelled as thick elements with a sufficient mesh, to avoid problems with too coarse shells.  Floor 
slabs, slabbands for the parking, shear walls and retaining walls are all model as shell elements, 5 - 
Structural Description list the material properties. The following Table 9, Table 10,  
 
Table 11 and Table 12 list the different structural shell elements are their thickness with the 
corresponding material name.

The beams and columns were modelled as frame elements with the base material, and the cross-
section was imported from the AutoCAD drawings, in total 85 different beam and columns, mostly 
columns were defined in the FE-Models to create the model as close to the real structure as possible. 
The section properties for the columns and beams are provided in Appendix E - Linear Analysis 
Results. 
 
The foundation was modelled as spring elements for both the model with and without the basement. 
The model without the basement was considered entirely fixed at the concrete core, and the columns 
have limited rotational stiffness. The model with the basement have springs placed 200 mm under the 
base slab and along the height of the basement to model the soil-structure interaction. The springs 
stiffness is set to 1e11 kN-m for transversal and 1e11 kN-m/rad for rotational for the entirely fixed 
support. The values are considered to be sufficient to model rigid behaviour [28]. The springs along 
==PAGE:86==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
68 
 
the height of the retaining walls have no rotational stiffness or stiffness against vertical movements 
in the Global Z direction. 
Structural details regarding the concrete core, generally the door openings in the concrete core is 2.2 
m in height above the floor slab. The windows in the South concrete core located at level 28th and 
29th, is located 957 mm above the floor slab to bottom of the window, and the distance the top of the 
window to the slab above is 957 mm.

modelling process. Figure 37 illustrates the two numerical models in SAP 2000.

An investigation of the modal response of the FE-Models with and without the basement was 
performed and compared the difference between the estimated frequencies with the Eigen and the 
Ritz approach. Table 13 shows for the modal response of the FE-model with the basement, the 
difference between the frequencies for the different estimation approaches either Eigen or Ritz was 
neglectable. The results of the Ritz vector approach is an approximation, and the solution is sensitive 
to the selected number of Ritz vectors, for this analysis, 45 Ritz vectors were applied. When applying 
a smaller number of Ritz vectors for the analysis, the differences in frequencies increases. The modal 
mass is similar for the two estimation approaches; the listed values were for the Eigenanalysis, 
Appendix E - Linear Analysis Results shows the modal masses for the Ritz vectors.  
 
The modal mass summation increase relatively slowly, and after 12 modes the mass participation is 
only around 55.3 % for the X (East-West) direction and 56.26 % for the Y (North-South) direction. 
These values are around 2/3 of the recommended value from FEMA 356, which is 90% of the total 
mass, must be included in the response spectrum analysis, to determine if the higher modes are 
==PAGE:91==
Linear Finite Element Model Analysis 
 
73 
 
significant for the structural response, [10]. A likely explanation for the modal mass participation 
ratios is the response of the basement is not in the range of 0 - 10 Hz, as shown at Figure 38, Figure 
39, Figure 40, Figure 41, Figure 42 and Figure 43 the basement does not participate in these mode 
shapes. Due to the size of the basement, when its participation in a mode shape, the modal mass 
increases. The increase in the modal mass was observed from 22.7 Hz, and above was the basement 
starts to participate in the mode shapes. In 10 estimates of the Ritz, mode shapes the mass participation 
ratio increase from 61 % to 92% in the X (East-West) direction and from 61 % to 93 % in the Y 
(North-South) direction. The higher modes, above 10 Hz, is essential for the global response of the 
structure, [18]. It is worth noting the first two-mode shape in the longitudinal direction North-South 
and East-West are closely spaced 0.49 Hz, and 0.61 Hz compared the first torsional mode shape at 
1.44 Hz.  
 
Different boundary constraints were applied to investigate the response, also the soil springs at the 
retaining walls. The soil springs at the retaining walls had a limited influence on the global response, 
but the springs applied to simulate the foundation plates had a significant influence on the global 
response.

7.3 Numerical results – Local Model 
The local mode shape of the second floor was estimated with the Eigen and Ritz method. SAP200 
estimates more than 500 Eigenmodes in the interval between 0 – 18 Hz, due to local mode shapes of 
the individual slabs and shell elements. The author decided to locate the mode shape with the Ritz 
vectors method and then try to find it among the many Eigenfrequencies. The first out of plan bending 
==PAGE:93==
Linear Finite Element Model Analysis 
 
75 
 
mode shape of the second floor was estimated at 14.20 Hz with the Ritz method, and the mode shapes 
are illustrated at Figure 44, First bending mode shape of the second floor. With this Ritz frequency it 
was attempted to local the corresponding Eigenfrequency, but since this was extremely time 
consuming and it was not possible to local a mode shapes with a similar frequency as the Ritz, the 
author decided not to estimate the Eigenfrequency for the bending mode shapes manual. The 
procedure for the automatic estimation of the local mode shape is explained in section 8.2 - Local 
Model

This section includes a comparison of the experimental Ambient Vibration Test and the numerical 
Finite Element results, model updating of the FE-Models and investigating the influence of the

8.1 Global Models 
The frequency response of the first 12 global modes for the two built FE-Models without and with 
the basement respectively, are shown in Table 15. The table lists the frequencies from the FE-Models 
with the corresponding Eigenfrequencies. Looking at the first three modes, North-South 1 (NS 1), 
East-West 1 (EW 1) and the first Torsional (T1), the frequency response of the FE-Model without the 
basement is closer to the experimental analysis, than for the FE-Model with the basement. The 
response difference for mode shapes NS 1 and EW1 for the FE-Model with and without the basement, 
are approximately 10 % points. This increase in difference is explained by introducing the basement 
since the total structural height increases with 9.6 m giving a more flexible structure, and a change in 
stiffness at the interface between the high-rise and the basement. The effect of increasing the height 
of the structure affects the longitudinal mode shapes, but not significantly the Torsional mode shapes 
where the difference was relatively low 1.6 % point. Remembering the general formulation of the 
frequency is the square root of the stiffness divided by the mass, and the basement adds a significant 
more mass to structure and stiffness. In the previous section 7 - Linear Finite Element Model Analysis, 
where the modal participation ratios were around 38 % with the basement and 60 % without for the 
corresponding NS 1 and EW1 mode shapes. Adding the basement also introduces additional stiffness 
to the system, but a critical stiffness is the stiffness of the interface between the high-rise and the top 
slab of the basement. For the FE-Model without the basement, this interface stiffness is assumed rigid, 
where for the FE-Model with the basement the stiffness of the interface is estimated by the FE-Model. 
As an additional study, the top slab for the basement was assumed entirely fixed with the same spring 
stiffness as for the base slab, and global frequency response of the structure was approaching the FE-
Model without the basement, as expected. It is expected to see a difference in the frequency response 
of the two FE-Models.  
 
The for the first six modes for both FE-Models the larges differences were the Torsional modes 
shapes, which is a standard case because the Torsional stiffness often involves more uncertainty in 
==PAGE:95==
Comparing Experimental and Numerical Results 
 
77 
 
the modelling phase. The estimations for the longitudinal mode shapes were closer to the 
experimental values as expected since the stiffness of the longitudinal modes were easy to model.  
 
An odd result from Table 15 was the change in positive and negative sigs of the frequency difference, 
this is not common to see, and should not happen. Looking at NS 1 and NS 2 for the FE-Model 
without the basement, the difference change from -5.7 % to 6.4 %. This change in sign does not 
physically make sense; for a further discussion of this issue see section 9 - Discussion.  The result is 
an indication of inconsistency between the real structure and the FE-Models.
