8.2 Local Model 
Estimating of the local mode shapes for the second-floor caused issues when estimated with the Eigen 
method, due to a large number of modes. The software FEMtools was used to estimate the MAC 
value of the mode shapes and pair the corresponding the frequencies. Unfortunately due to the second 
floor only had four measurement points, along one line in one direction, the results were inferior from 
the FEMtools analysis. The program pair mode shapes with a difference in frequency of more than 
100 % and multiple FE mode shapes have a MAC value of above 80 % for the same experimental 
mode shape. The results of this analysis are not shown in this thesis. A conclusion from this analysis 
of the local mode shape was the four sensors in one line was insufficient to have a precise estimation 
of the mode shape. The SSI – UPC method estimated the local mode shape to 12.35 Hz, and it was 
attempted to search for the local mode shape from the FE-Model frequency response but, the mode 
shapes was not possible to locate among the Eigenmode shapes. With the Ritz method, the first 
bending mode shape was estimated to 14.20 Hz, corresponding to a 15 % difference compared to the 
experimental frequency. 
 
8.3 Diaphragm Assumptions 
The assumptions for the floor diaphragms were studied, how the different assumptions in stiffness of 
the floor diaphragm influence the global response of the structure. There are several different ways 
of modelling diaphragms in SAP2000, either the diaphragm is flexible, semi-rigid or rigid. The 
==PAGE:97==
Comparing Experimental and Numerical Results 
 
79 
 
flexible diaphragm is not a constraint thus the software does not lump any masses or reduces the 
number of nodes, when shell elements are used for the diaphragm these are seen flexible, meaning 
SAP2000 compute the stiffness of the shell, without modifying it. The accidental eccentricity 
associated the seismic loads, will not be applied to any diaphragm nodes. For the semi-rigid constraint 
SAP2000 compute the real in-plan stiffness properties and behaviour, and for seismic loads, the 
accidental eccentricity is applied to every node in the shell. The rigid diaphragm assumption has 
opposite the two other assumption infinite in-plane stiffness properties. The rigid constraint lump the 
masses at the centre of the diaphragm, which condense the stiffness matrix and enhance the 
computation time. For seismic loads, the accidental eccentricity is applied to the centre of mass for 
the diaphragm, [32].  
 
When engineers design structures, the assumptions of the diaphragms is, in general, a tricky question, 
because in a design office the engineer does not have access to the actual response data of the 
structure. Choosing the right assumption is rather important for the structural response and 
computational time of the analysis. Table 17 illustrates the structural response of the three different 
floor diaphragm assumptions from FE-Model with the basement. Each floor is model as one 
diaphragm. The table illustrates the rigid diaphragm assumption is not valid for this structure. The 
difference is 36 - 39 %, more than twice the difference of the flexible and semi-rigid diaphragm 
assumptions with an absolute difference value of 6 - 16 %. The rigid diaphragm assumption creates 
artificially a too high diaphragm stiffness, which increases the response frequency for the structure. 
The semi-rigid and flexible assumptions are significantly closer to the response of the real structure. 
Both the semi-rigid and the flexible assumption have nearly identical results, meaning the diaphragms 
have significant in-plane deformations. The increase of the frequency response for the rigid 
assumption seems to affect the longitudinal mode shapes more, compared to the Torsional one, for 
the first order of mode shapes. The NS 1 increase with 54.8 % points from -15.7 % to 39.1 % and the 
EW 1 increase with 41.7 % point from -5.9 % to 35.8 %, where T1 only increase with 24.2 % points 
from 14.1 % to 38.3 %.  
It is worth noting the frequency response difference between the three diaphragm assumptions are 
minimal for the 2-order mode shapes in longitudinal directions North-South and East-West. The 
difference in Torsional is still significant between rigid and flexible/semi-rigid.

8.4 Sensitivity Analysis 
Finite Element Models are always an approximation of the real structure, and differences between 
the FE-Models and the results of the Ambient Vibration Test are expected. Model updating is a tool 
==PAGE:100==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
82 
 
to minimise the differences between the FE-model and the AVT result. Before performing the model 
updating, it is essential to consider the uncertainties of the FE-model. Connections and supports are 
associated with a relatively high uncertainty compared to the material density and Young’s modulus 
for well-defined materials as steel. According to Brincker and Ventura, [15], their research and 
experience in FE-model updating and critical physical quantities versus levels of uncertainty are 
illustrated in Table 20.  
Table 20, The most critical parameter of FE-model updating and the level of uncertainty, from [15]. 
Physical quantity 
Level of uncertainty (%)

metals 
1-5

fibre-reinforced materials 
5-20 
Boundary conditions to the ground 
10-infinity 
Joints bolted 
10-infinity 
Joints, welded 
2-10 
Total mass 
1-5 
Estimated natural frequency 
0.1-0.5 
Measured response 
0.2-2 
Estimated mode shapes 
2-5 
Estimated damping 
5-20 
Mode shape scaling factor 
5-20 
 
8.4.1 Global Sensitivity Analysis 
A sensitivity analysis was conducted, before updating the FE-Model to the ambient test results. The 
analysis was performed for both the structures with and without the basement, to observe if the models 
showed any difference in sensitivity. The sensitivity analysis clarifies the effects of stiffness and mass 
modification, compared to the entire structural response. The analysis will clarify the response 
sensitivity to changes in the stiffness and mass. The sensitivity analysis was selected for the stiffness 
and mass density, because the high-rise is a reinforced concrete tower, and the soil is class C, meaning 
firm soil, creating relative stiff support for the structure. The changes in the stiffness of the FE-Model 
were performed by changing the Elasticity Young’s modulus, since the linear relationship, between 
the cross-section area A, length L, Young’s modulus and the stiffness k.  
 
 
𝑘= 𝐴𝐸
𝐿
 
 
(8.1) 
The shear modulus G, the Torsional section moment J and the length define the rotational stiffness. 
==PAGE:101==
Comparing Experimental and Numerical Results 
 
83 
 
 
 
𝑘= 𝐺𝐽
𝐿 
 
(8.2) 
The well know relation for anisotropic material between the shear modulus the elasticity modulus, 
[33] 
 
 
𝐸= 2𝐺(1 + 𝜈) 
 
(8.3) 
Figure 45 and Figure 46 illustrates the results of the sensitivity analysis for the model with and without 
the basement respectively. The figures illustrate an approximated linear relationship between the 
frequency and the reduction of stiffness in first frequencies in direction North-South, East-West. For 
the Torsional mode, the curve shows a linear tendency, but the curve changes slope along the line. 
The two longitudinal modes vary with a constant and low slope value, meaning the modes are not 
very sensitive to stiffness changes in the concrete. Comparing the slope of the longitudinal mode with 
the slope of the Torsional mode, the slope was significantly steeper for the Torsional mode, and 
consequently, mode shape was sensitive to changes in stiffness of the concrete. Increasing the 
concrete stiffness increases the frequency of the fundamental modes as expected. Figure 45 and 
Figure 46 illustrates the longitudinal modes shapes were closely spaced, and the frequency of the 
Torsional mode is approximately twice the longitudinal mode shapes. Comparing the two models 
with and without the basement, with figures shows the frequencies of the longitudinal mode shapes 
decreases with the basement. The frequency change in the Torsional mode shape is minimal, the 
numeric change in value is 0.03 Hz from 1.43 Hz with basement to 1.46 Hz without the basement. 
Thus present in the basement does not affect the Torsional mode shape significantly but does affect 
the longitudinal mode shape significantly.

the basement is only significant for the longitudinal mode shapes. The correlation of the stiffness for 
the three first modes shapes tended to an exponentially decreasing function.

Models have for the first six modes a MAC value above 90 %, which is satisficing considering the 
complexity and irregularities of the structure. Regarding the sensitivity analysis, the results show a 
similar sensitivity about mass density and Young’s modulus for both models. The same trend was 
observed in Figure 45, Figure 46, Figure 47 and Figure 48. The two FE-Models are distinctly different 
regarding the modal mass participation factors; a summation of the first six modes shapes for the FE-
Model with the basement shows a factor of only 50 % of the mass participation in the first six modes. 
Comparing with the FE-Model without the basement, the summation of the modal mass participation 
factor is 80 % for the first six modes. FEMA 356 states the modal mass participation factor would be 
at least 90 %, to account for the importance of the higher modes, [10]. The FE-Model with the 
basement will be notably heavier in computation power for the model to reach the 90 %, where the 
FE-Model without the basement will require fewer modes to reach this value. 
 
An important parameter to mention when deciding the importance of the basement in the analysis is 
the soil-structure interaction; this influences global structural response because the soil around the 
basement acts as springs, restraining the retaining walls of the basement with a stiffness. This stiffness 
is often very hard to estimate with limited or without any site measurements of the soil stiffness. The 
building is built on the firm ground (NBCC 2015 soil class C); with an average Vs30 shear wave 
velocity at 450 m/s, [26]. The difference between the fundamental period of the site and the structure 
can determine if the soil-structure interaction is of greater importance. If the fundamental period of 
the site is longer than the fundamental period of the structure, accounting for soil-structure interaction 
in the FE model is prudent. In the case, the fundamental period of the site is less than the fundamental 
period of the structure; then the SsI will reduce the response of the structure. The fundamental period 
of the structure is 1.75 s corresponding to 0.57 Hz. The estimated site period is the depth of the soil 
stratum 𝐻 divided by the shear wave velocity of the soil 𝑉𝑠, [11]. The depth of the soil stratum is not 
known, but estimating the upper bound value as  
 
 
𝐻= 𝑇𝑠⋅𝑉𝑠
4
= 1.75𝑠⋅450 𝑚/𝑠
4
= 197𝑚 
 
(8.4) 
The soil-structure interaction influence for this structure is assumed limited because the soil class C 
for firm soil, and thus the soil-structure interaction from the incident waves from an earthquake source 
can be assumed to have minimal effect for these types of structures. The depth of the bedrock is 
considered lower than 197 m. Thus a fixed base structure is a realistic result of this site. 
==PAGE:105==
Comparing Experimental and Numerical Results 
 
87 
 
The conclusion of this part section is, from this point and forward, the FE-Model without the 
basement is the model which represents the real structure to the highest degree, regarding the above-
discussed parameters, and FE-Model with the basement will not. The referred baseline model is FE-
Model without the basement. 
 
8.6 Model Updating 
Mode updating is a handy tool to understand where the baseline FE-Model is different from the real 
structure. For this study, the commercial software FEMTools 4.0.1 was applied to perform all 
automated updating of the FE-Models. As Table 20 in section 8.4 - Sensitivity Analysis illustrates, 
the estimation of specific parameters is correlated with high uncertainty. For this study, the mass 
density, Young's modulus and the spring stiffness of the boundary conditions, were chosen as the 
three main parameters for the updating.  
The first step of the updating process was the investigate the importance of changing mass density 
and Young's modulus for the column versus the concrete core. The results from manual updating 
showed as expected; the concrete core has a significant impact on the global response of the structure 
compared to the columns. The concrete core discretisation went from five different materials to eight 
materials as illustrated in Table 21, the thickness of the materials of the strength was not changed.

8.6.1.1 Young’s Modulus Reduction 
This updating focus on the changes in Young’s modulus of the baseline model. The automated 
updating program was coded to perform ten iterations with a maximum step of 2 % of the parameter 
value and find a closer match with the frequencies and the MAC values. Figure 52 illustrates the 
parameter tracking from FEMTools, and the larges parameter changes include parameter 1-4, as 
expected from the sensitivity analysis thus these parameters were essential for the frequency response. 
The stiffness of the baseline FE-Model is too high, and the parameters were reduced from the baseline 
values. A reduction of Young’s modulus of 16 % is a significant change, and updating of only 
Young’s modulus might not be sufficient solely. The time elapsed for the automated updating process 
was 4 hours and 14 minutes.

Young’s modulus with 16 % introduces a significant improvement of the East-West and the Torsional 
mode shapes, the difference decrease of 5.3 and 5.9 % points respectively. The stiffness reduction 
increased the difference of the first North-South mode shape with 2.1 % points. Changing of the 
spring stiffness had the most significant impact in the first North-South mode shape, the difference

==PAGE:115==
Discussion 
 
97 
 
 
Norman D. Schuster recorded the Ambient Vibration Test data used for the Operational Modal 
Analysis part of this study. When applying data from other researchers, it is essential to know the 
execution of the experiments. Field comments from the measurements are essential too, what 
happened during the test, were there any obstacles. Ideally, the data should be verified thought 
publications. Norman D. Schuster recorded the data under supervision by professor Carlos Ventura 
at UBC. Furthermore, the data are published as a journal paper with the title “Structural dynamic 
properties of a reinforced concrete high-rise building during construction” in Canadian Journal of 
Civil Engineering, [34]. The data was also the basis of the PhD Thesis by Norman D. Schuster with 
the title “Dynamic Characteristics of a 30 Storey Building During Construction Detected From 
Ambient Vibration Measurements” [24]. The ambient vibration data was presented in the book 
“Introduction To Operational Modal Analysis” by Brincker and Ventura, [15] and the paper “Ground 
motion sensitivity of a Vancouver-style high rise” by White and Ventura, [35]. Furthermore, field 
observations very detailed in the PhD Thesis by Norman D. Schuster. The author had several concerns 
regarding applying the data. First, a high pass filter on site filtered the obtained measurements data. 
Second, the duration of the Ambient Vibration Test was relatively short 13 minutes and 39 seconds. 
The duration was still above the requirement regarding the maximum correlation time for the response 
by allowing at least 20 data segments to estimate a reasonable correlation function of the response. 
However, it is best practice today to acquire measurements for 20-30 minutes for similar structures, 
to have greater confidence in the results. The above confided the author confided the obtained data 
were of good quality and the data was applicable.  
 
The performed Operational Modal Analysis in ARTeMIS for the global structure had a geometry 
similar to the real structure with some simplifications; however, estimation of the mode shapes for 
every floor of the structure included implementation of several non-measurement nodes. Applying 
the rigid kinematic constraint by introducing slaved nodes, the non-measurement nodes would 
participate in the mode shape. Remembering the overall amid of the thesis is to investigate the 
behaviour of the floor slabs, the slave nodes must be used with reservations since it applies a 
kinematic constraint. The kinematic constraint does not affect the frequencies; they were estimated 
solely from the measurements data. The decision to apply the kinematic constraint were quantified 
because each measurement node did not have measurements for both X – and Y – directions. Giving 
==PAGE:116==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
98 
 
a model without the constraints would result in a non-realistic response, since two the single axis 
measurements nodes per floor, would only show modal deflection in that direction. In the ideal world, 
each sensor would have measurements data from both the X – and Y – directions, and a model without 
slave nodes. This test setup is not always affordable financially for all Ambient Vibration Test, and 
slaved node are in practice unavoidable. Slaving each non-measurement node to three other nodes, 
improved the OMA results, by not implementing the traditional one master node technique. The MAC 
analysis did show a higher correlation between experimental and numerical FE-Model by assuming 
flexible diaphragms, and not the rigid diaphragms. The results are inserting because the behaviour of 
the floor diaphragm in the experimental model was a rigid motion. It is satisfying to see the results of 
the experimental model was not significantly biased from the assumed rigid motion.   
 
The comparison of the experimental and numerical FE results illustrated odd results for the 
frequencies, a change in sign between positive and negative. It is not common to observe this 
phenomenon, and it was odd to see. Evaluating mode shapes North-South 1 and North-South 2 for 
the FE-Model without the basement, the difference changed from -5.7 % to 6.4 %, this change in sign 
does not make sense physically. The observed tendency was identical for the FE-Model with the 
basement; where the mode shapes North-South 2 and 3 had a frequency difference of -2.2 % to 5.2 
% and East-West 2 and 3 had a frequency difference of -0.8% to 7.6 %. A structure cannot have a 
low stiffness at the first mode shape and then have a high stiffness for the next mode shape; this 
indicates there is an inconsistency between the FE-Models and the real structure. There is something, 
which is not built as planned or an imperfection that creates this difference. The stiffness difference 
giving the change in signs are also seen in the results from the study by Schuster [24], but not further 
addressed.  
 
A possible explanation for the sign chance is stiffness imperfections, and the monolithic stiffness 
thought out the height if the structure is interrupted. The updating of the structure gave grounds for a 
stiffness related issues. The frequency differences were improved by reducing the stiffness from the 
ground floor to the 20th floor, and the two most substantial observed stiffness changes were at ground 
floor to the 5th floor and floor 10th to 15th. The issue is possibly due to construction errors, during 
casting reinforcement between two casted sections were insufficient or missing. The engineers on site 
decided to drill the reinforcement into the semi harden concrete, creating small cracks and reducing 
the connectivity of the reinforcement significantly. If this is the case, the stiffness is non-monolithic 
==PAGE:117==
Discussion 
 
99 
 
thought out the structure but has particular gaps along the height. The non-monolithic stiffness would 
introduce local rocking of the concrete core, which is a non-linear problem, and it is not expected the 
updated linear mode captures this behaviour.  
 
The stiffness issue of the concrete core is possible to investigate by performing two new full Ambient 
Vibration Test of the current condition of the high-rise. One test is not enough because the already 
obtained data was solely from the construction phase. By performing, both AVTs at significantly 
different wind speeds the Operational Modal Analysis might show different results, due to this 
localised rocking phenomenon. Ideally, the OMA results should be identical for the linear case, but 
if there is local rocking of the core, the frequencies might change, because the rocking is a non-linear 
phenomenon.  Additional to the two new AVT test a visual inspection of the core joints at the floor 
is advised, it will be possible to find small cracks and dust if the local rocking occurs. The essential 
question is how can we fix or improve this issue with the concrete core. A cost-effective solution is 
to investigate the possibility to introduce post-tensioning cables spanning from the top of the core to 
the bottom. The cables can run in the service channels in the concrete core. The solution is 
implemented physically by having two large anchor beams one in the top and one in the basement, 
and then tension the cables. For this specific building, the service channels are close lift shaft to the 
middle of the concrete core, and it might be necessary to drill new holes along the concrete core walls 
for the post-tension cables.  
 
The City Crest Tower is located in the most active seismic region of Canada, British Columbia, at the 
Cascadia fault where three types of earthquakes occur: crustal earthquakes, sub-crustal earthquake 
and subduction earthquakes. Designing structures within seismic areas have to resist the loads from 
the strong ground motions, and the fundamental period of the structure has a significant influence on 
the seismic design loads. Figure 58 illustrates the Uniform Hazard Spectrum with a 2 % in 50 years 
probability of exceedance for the exact location of the City Crest Tower; the spectrum is estimated 
from Natural Resources Canada, [26]. The figure illustrates the fundamental period of the structure 
with the different diaphragm assumptions and the corresponding spectrum accelerations. Appling, 
the rigid diaphragm assumption, leads to a significant higher spectrum acceleration approximately 
0.4 g, where the flexible assumption leads to a spectrum acceleration of 0.25 g. The OMA results lead 
to a spectrum acceleration of 0.27 g. It is evident when applying the rigid diaphragm assumption this 
lead to an incorrect fundamental period for this structure, and the baseline model for the seismic 
==PAGE:118==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
100 
 
design is significantly different from the real structure. Seismic design is not solely based on the floor 
diaphragms, the concrete core has a major importance for the design, several safety factors are applied 
to ensure redundancy of the structural system.  
 
Figure 58, UHS Spectrum for the City Crest Tower with the fundamental periods.

==PAGE:119==
Conclusion 
 
101 
 
 
The outcomes of this comprehensive research master’s thesis are listed in this section and divided 
into three main subjects: The Experimental Results, The Linear Finite Element Results and The 
Linear Finite Element Results. The final part of this section includes a perspective for future 
investigations. 
 
The Experimental Results   
The Ambient Vibrations Test of the City Crest Tower was the basis of the dynamic parameter 
estimation for this thesis. The performed operational modal analysis estimated 12 global mode shapes 
of the global structure and one mode shape of the extension of the second. Both the EFDD and SSI – 
UPC methods were applied to estimate the mode shapes, frequencies and damping ratio. The 
frequency difference between the two estimates was limited. However, the damping difference was 
significant. The estimated damping value was within a similar damping range for both estimations 
methods, 0.8 to 3.5 % for the EFDD method and 0.9 to 3.4 % for the SSI – UPC method. The SSI – 
UPC method estimates the frequencies for the first mode shapes in North-South was 0.57 Hz, East-
West 0.64 Hz and Torsional 1.26 Hz; the first 12 modes were within the range of 0-10 Hz. For the 
local structure of the second floor, one mode shape was estimated at 12.35 Hz with a corresponding 
damping ratio of 3.21 %. 
 
The Linear Finite Element Results  
The dynamic parameters of the different linear Finite Element models with and without the basement 
were investigated. It was evident the mode shapes for the FE-Models with and without the basements 
were similar. With visual inspection, the basement did not participate in the first 12 modes between 
0 and 10 Hz, which the mass participation ratios showed too. The FE-Model with the basement had 
a slowly increasing modal mass participation factor, meaning the higher modes, above 10 Hz, was 
essential for the global response of the structure. The FE-model without the basement had a rapid 
increasing modal mass participation factor from mode 1 to 5 where X - and Y - direction reach a 
cumulative sum of 80 % in the modal mass participation. After 12 modes the cumulative modal mass 
participation reaches 90 %, which is partly due to the several numbers of irregularities of the structure. 
 
==PAGE:120==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
102 
 
The dynamic parameters of the second floor were obtained, and the first bending mode shape of the 
second floor at 14.20 Hz estimate with the Ritz method. It was not possible to manual estimate the 
Eigenfrequency from the FE-program. 
 
Comparison of The Experimental and The Numerical Results 
The presence of the basement was significantly affecting the frequencies of the longitudinal mode 
shapes approximately 10 % points. However, the effect was negligible for the torsional mode shape 
around 1.6 % point in response difference. The two FE-models with and without the basement 
captured the behaviour of the mode shapes to an excellent degree; the first six modes all had a MAC 
value above 90 %, a perfect correlation, considering the complexity and irregularities of the structure.  
 
The floor diaphragm assumptions of this structure had significant influences on the global structural 
response. The effect of assuming flexible, semi-rigid and rigid diaphragms were studied, for this a 
single concrete core structure with multiple irregularities. The flexible diaphragm assumption leads 
to an absolute frequency difference of 6 – 16 % for the first three modes, for the FE-Model with the 
basement compared to the experimental results. The rigid assumption lead to an infinite in-plane floor 
stiffness, and thus the frequency response difference increased to 38 - 39 %, above the experimental 
test results. The first three modes shapes including the longitudinal mode shapes were significantly 
more sensitive to the diaphragm assumptions either flexible or rigid compared to the torsional mode 
shape.  
 
For the FE-Model without the basement, the flexible diaphragms assumption lead to an absolute 
frequency difference of 6 – 16 % for the first three modes, compared to the experimental results. The 
rigid diaphragm assumption resulted in a frequency response difference of more than 42-54 %.  As 
for the FE-Model with the basement, for the first three modes shapes the longitudinal mode shapes 
were significantly more sensitive to the diaphragm assumptions compared to the torsional mode 
shapes. The frequency response increased and for the 1st North-South mode shape with 60.0 % points 
from -5.7 % (flexible) to 54.3 % (rigid) and the 1st East-West mode shape with 44.7 % points from 
7.1 % (flexible) to 51.8 % (rigid). The 1st Torsional mode shape increased with 26.2 % points from 
15.9 % (flexible) to 42.1 % (rigid). The frequency response of the semi-rigid diaphragms was nearly 
identical to the flexible response for the both the FE-Model with and without the basement.  The 
MAC values shown a significant drop in correlation when applying the rigid diaphragm assumption 
==PAGE:121==
Conclusion 
 
103 
 
compared to the flexible assumption. The MAC values were 10-20 % points lower for the rigid 
diaphragm assumptions than the flexible diaphragm assumption, which MAC values were between 
95 % and 98 % for the first three modes. 
 
The modal updating analysis included the FE-Model solely without the basement because of the 
limited influence of the soil-structure interaction due to the firm soil. The conclusion of the modal 
updating showed the concrete core walls were of high importance as expected, and the inner concrete 
walls had a low influence on the global response when reducing the stiffness significantly. The 
external shear wall had the minimal influence on the global response. The frequency response was 
sensitive to changes in Young's modulus from level 1 to 20. Appling the spring stiffness reduction 
and limited the reduction of Young’s modulus to 12 %, when the frequency difference of the first 
North-South mode shape increased 0.7 % points. However, the East-West and the torsional mode 
shape decreased by 3.7 % points and 3.7 % % points respectively.  
 
Recommendations for Future Investigations  
Future research studies could assess the diaphragm effect of the dynamic response for a linear and a 
non-linear FE-model with investigating strong ground motions. It would be interesting to study the 
deformations along the diaphragms and inter-storey shear forces for the different diaphragm 
assumptions. Also performing a comprehensive comparison with the building codes, to evaluate if 
the codes estimation of rigid or flexible diaphragm behaviour is valid. 
 
An investigation of the stress distributions of the diaphragms during strong ground motions, and study 
the influence of reinforcement along the edge of the diaphragms how they affect the structural 
response during strong ground motions. The stress distribution around the concrete core is a possible 
research topic when the applying different diaphragm assumptions.

Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance

Preliminary results of this study were presented at the 1st China-Canada Symposium on Structural & 
Earthquake Engineering (CCSSEE) as a three minutes thesis presentation. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:127==
 
The 1st China-Canada Symposium on Structural and Earthquake Engineering 
August 20-24 2017, Vancouver, Canada

Niels Juhl Savnik 
Master student, University of British Columbia, Canada and Technical University of Denmark, Denmark  
S123685@student.dtu.dk 
Carlos E. Ventura

ventura@civil.ubc.ca

Freddy Pina  
Dr, PBRV Consulting Ltd., Canada  
fpina@pbrvconsulting.com  
Keywords: Ambient Vibration; Operational Modal Analysis; Diaphragm performance; FEM updating;  
Objectives 
A large number of existing structures as buildings, 
bridges and other infrastructures, have decreasing 
performance, and it is very uncertain to predict the 
capacity with confidence for the rest of the service life. 
Based on this, it is a high priority for the engineering 
society to identify the real performance of structures, 
when they are exposed to different types of effects. 
This is the only way to precisely predict the current 
capacity of the structure, and evaluate the opportunity 
for retrofitting, if necessary. Within this framework, 
the aim of this master project is to identify the 
horizontal movements of existing reinforced concrete 
structures exposed to wind loads, earthquake loads and 
ambient vibrations. Primary there is a gap in the 
literature related to studies concentrate on horizontal 
movements of diaphragms, and this incomplete 
knowledge have already lead to simplified models, 
which 
cannot 
predict 
the 
precise 
horizontal 
movements.  Thereby is it anticipated, this integrated 
research project, which includes both an experimental 
and numerical part, will contribute with the necessary 
knowledge to predict the horizontal movements of a 
building.  More specific the aim of the research project 
is to understand the horizontal behavior of high-rise 
buildings, by investigating the movement of the 
horizontal diaphragms, and performing a nonlinear 
dynamic analysis of the storey diaphragms. The 
emphasis of the project is to clarify the diaphragm 
effect in relation to horizontal behaviour in high-rise 
buildings. The investigation of the diaphragm effect 
embrace acquiring ambient vibration accelerations 
from the horizontal movement of a high-rise building, 
placed in Vancouver Canada, and comparison with a 
comprehensive highly nonlinear 3D Finite Element 
Model of the high-rise building. A part of the 
preliminary work contains a careful study of the 
modelling process and the Rayleigh-Ritz method. The 
diaphragms are modelled as shell elements with 
nonlinear layers. The dynamic properties of the 
structure, as modes and frequencies, are obtained from 
the ambient vibrations with Operational Modal 
Analysis, and for the 3D FE Model by applying the 
Rayleigh-Ritz method. Time history analysis of 
known strong ground motions are performed to 
investigate the response of the 3D FE Model, and 
study the capturing of the diaphragm effect. Finally, a 
comprehensive 
study 
comparing 
the 
captured 
diaphragm effect with the level of detailing of the 
diaphragms in the 3D FE model are performed.  
 
 
==PAGE:128==
Page 2 of 2 
The investigated building 
The building selected for this study is The City Crest 
Towner in downtown Vancouver, Canada, the 
building is a high-rise with flat slab and edge columns 
with one concrete core and very limit shear walls. The 
low shear wall ratio enhance the possibility to 
investigate the diaphragm movement, and avoid the 
measurement being biased from shear walls. It is of 
interested to investigate this type of buildings, because 
the real repose of the diaphragm is simply not known. 
The diaphragm is often simplified to be a rigid 
element, but the engineers are missing research where 
experimental and numerical result are compared to 
validate this assumption.  
The City Crest Tower is a 32-storey podium 
tower with multiple irregularities and especially the 
setback at level 10. The floor plan of the tower is non-
symmetry, and the outer dimensions for a general floor 
are 28 m x 24.7 m, see Figure 1. At the second floor, 
the floor is extended with a 10.8 m x 18 m terrace, 
which is of special interested for this study. The 
instrumentation of the building was performed in the 
summer of 1993, for the terrace setup four sensors 
were place along a line in the middle of the diaphragm, 
DQGWKHVHQVRU¶VPHDVXUHPHQWGLUHFWLRQLVRUWKRJRQDO
to the sensor line. 
 
  
Figure 1, left: floor plan of the City Crest Tower.  
Performance of the research 
Through 
ambient 
vibrations 
and 
performing 
Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) the dynamics 
properties for both the diaphragm and the global 
structure are determined. The methods used to 
determine the frequencies and damping are Enhanced 
Frequency-Domain Decomposition (EFFD) and a Sto-
chastic Subspace Identification (SSI) in the software 
program ARTeMIS Modal Pro. In order to isolate the 
dynamic modelling of the diaphragm and the global 
structure, the 3D FE Model is a twostep procedure.  
First, the diaphragm is modelled and the 
dynamic properties are determined with Rayleigh-Ritz 
method. The diaphragm model is updated to match the 
OMA results, by changing different parameters of the 
diaphragm. The Modal Assurance Criterion is a key 
method to match the modes from the real response and 
the FE Model.  
The second step is to model the global 
structure with the updated diaphragm model. When 
updating the global model to match the OMA results, 
WKH GLDSKUDJP ZLOO QRW EH FKDQJHG 7KLV ZD\ LW¶V
possible to exam the diaphragm and the global 
structure as two isolated problems.  
 
A comprehensive 3D FE Model, is not always perform 
when designing these structures, thus this research will 
look into how detailed should engineers model the 
diaphragms, to capture a satisfying behaviour. The 
diaphragm will be model in different detailed levels, 
and through a time history analysis of different strong 
ground motions, the responses from the different 
diaphragm models are investigated. 
 
OMA results 
The results from the Operational Modal Analysis 
shows the first modes in NS and EW are closed spaced 
at 0.57 Hz and 0.64 Hz with a damping of 2.88 % and 
3.41 % respectively. Frist torsional mode at 1.26 Hz 
with 2.31% damping. The damping ratios corresponds 
to the recommended literature values of 3-5 %, for 
reinforced concrete structure with working stress. 
Figure 2, illustrates the modes from the OMA, 14 
modes are determined, the modes in the range of 0-
10.5 Hz are associated with the entire structure, and 
the two modes above this range are associated to the 
diaphragm of the second floor. 
Figure 2, Singular Values of Spectral Densities. 
Future 
The future phase of this research project is to start the 
modelling phase in a FEM program and step into the 
modeling updating part.

Definition of variables  
All of the *.bbb files are stored in binary format. The data (in units of volts) is preceded by a header 
which identifies the file and includes all pertinent information.  Specific details of this file are given 
below.

#include<stdio.h>

/****************************************************************************/ 
 
void main( int argc, char *argv[] ) 
{ 
 
FILE 
*fp; 
 
char 
filename[50]; 
 
 
 
if( argc < 2 ) 
 
{ 
 
 
EchoInstructions(); 
 
 
exit(EXIT_SUCCESS); 
 
} 
 
 
sprintf( filename, "%s.bbb", argv[1] ); 
 
 
printf( "Opening input file: %s\n", filename ); 
 
if( (fp = fopen( filename, "rb" )) == NULL ) 
 
{ 
 
 
printf("Error opening file! -- Terminating Program.\n"); 
 
 
exit(EXIT_FAILURE); 
 
} 
 
 
printf( "Processing ...\n" ); 
 
ReadBBBFile( fp, argv[1] ); 
 
printf( "All done!\n"); 
}

Appendix C – C++ Code

/****************************************************************************/ 
 
void EchoInstructions() 
{ 
 
printf( 
 
"BBB2ASCI - (bbb-to-ascii) - 0.2.2\n" 
 
"\n" 
 
"This program translates a \"bbb\" binary file to an ascii file.  Note that it\n" 
 
"will only translate a file which was created using the UBC Kinemetrics/\n" 
 
"Keithley data acquisition system.  To translate a file, specify its name (no\n" 
 
"extension) on the command line when executing the program.\n" 
 
"\n" 
 
"eg. if the filename is \"corona03.bbb\", type the following at the DOS prompt:\n" 
 
"\n" 
 
" 
bbb2asci corona03\n" 
 
"\n" 
 
"The output file consists of a header file (with the extension \"hdr\"), as well\n" 
 
"as a separate file for each data segment, consisting of a single column of\n" 
 
"data.  The latter files will have an extension corresponsing to the segment\n" 
 
"number.  For example, if the above file consisted of 4 segments, then the\n" 
 
"following files will be created:\n" 
 
"\n" 
 
" 
corona03.hdr\n" 
 
" 
corona03.1\n" 
 
" 
corona03.2\n" 
 
" 
corona03.3\n" 
 
" 
corona03.4\n" 
 
); 
} 
 
/****************************************************************************/ 
 
void ReadBBBFile( FILE *fp, char *name ) 
{ 
 
long 
reserved; 
 
long 
segLen; 
// segment length 
Long 
N; 
// total number of points 
int16_t 
n, 
// number of points per segment 
 
mag; 
// global amplitude gain 
char 
filename[80], 
// filename - embedded in data 
 
site[80], 
// site 
 
setup[80], 
// setup 
 
id[50]; 
 
// identification string 
float 
del; 
// time increment 
struct date 
d; 
// date structure 
struct time 
t; 
// time structure 
 
 
 
==PAGE:150==
Experimental and Numerical Investigation on Existing Buildings’ Diaphragms Performance 
 
 
132 
 
long 
i; 
// counter 
int16_t 
j; 
// segment number (counter) 
float 
pt; 
// data point read from the file 
FILE 
*df; 
// pointer to ascii data file being 
generated 
FILE 
*hf; 
// pointer to header file being 
generated 
char 
file[50]; 
// name of ascii file being generated 
int16_t 
ns; 
// number of segments 
fpos_t 
filePos; 
// file position 
 
 
 
char 
BBBIdentifier[] = "Karg Weissbier ist das beste in der 
Welt"; 
 
// header file 
 
 
sprintf( file, "%s.hdr", name ); 
 
if( (hf = fopen( file, "w" )) == NULL ) 
 
{ 
 
 
printf("Error creating %s! -- Terminating Program\n", file ); 
 
 
exit(EXIT_FAILURE); 
 
} 
 
 
printf( "Reading header information...\n" ); 
 
fread( id, sizeof(char), 42, fp ); 
 
if( strcmp( id, BBBIdentifier ) != 0 ) 
 
{ 
 
 
printf("Invalid file format! -- Terminating Program\n" ); 
 
 
exit(EXIT_SUCCESS); 
 
} 
 
 
fread( &reserved, sizeof(long), 1, fp ); // empty 
 
 
printf( "embedded.reserved: %ld\n", reserved ); 
 
fread( &segLen, sizeof(long), 1, fp ); 
 
// segment length 
 
 
printf( "embedded.segLen  : %ld\n", segLen ); 
 
 
fread( &del, sizeof(float), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( &N, sizeof(long), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( &n, sizeof(int16_t), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( &mag, sizeof(int16_t), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( filename, sizeof(char), 80, fp ); 
 
fread( site, sizeof(char), 80, fp ); 
 
fread( setup, sizeof(char), 80, fp ); 
 
fread( &(d.da_year), sizeof(int16_t), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( &(d.da_day), sizeof(char), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( &(d.da_mon), sizeof(char), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( &(t.ti_hour), sizeof(unsigned char), 1, fp ); 
==PAGE:151==
Appendix C – C++ Code 
 
133 
 
 
fread( &(t.ti_min), sizeof(unsigned char), 1, fp ); 
 
fread( &(t.ti_sec), sizeof(unsigned char), 1, fp ); 
 
 
if( reserved != 0L ) 
 
 
segLen = 4096L; 
// default 
 
segLen = ( N < segLen ) ? N : segLen; 
 
 
ns = N/segLen; 
 
 
fprintf( hf, "               filename: %s\n", filename ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "                   site: %s\n", site ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "                  setup: %s\n\n", setup ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "         time increment: %f\n", del ); 
 
fprintf( hf, " total number of points: %ld\n", N ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "         segment length: %ld\n", segLen ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "     number of segments: %d\n", ns ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "   Keithley(tm) channel: %d\n", n ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "      Keithley(tm) gain: %d\n", mag ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "                   date: %d %s %d\n", d.da_year, months[d.da_mon], d.da_day 
); 
 
fprintf( hf, "                   time: %2u:%2u:%2u\n", t.ti_hour, t.ti_min, t.ti_sec ); 
 
fprintf( hf, "\n" ); 
 
// data 
 
 
for( j = 1; j <= ns; j++ ) 
 
{ 
 
 
printf("segment #%d\n", j); 
 
 
sprintf( file, "%s.%d", name, j ); 
 
 
if( (df = fopen( file, "w" )) == NULL ) 
 
 
{ 
 
 
 
printf("Error creating %s! -- Terminating Program\n", file 
); 
 
 
 
exit(EXIT_FAILURE); 
 
 
} 
// 
 
fgetpos( fp, &filePos ); 
// 
 
fprintf( hf, "file position - segment #%d: %ld\n", j, filePos ); 
 
 
for( i = 0; i < segLen; ++i ) 
 
 
{ 
 
 
 
printf("%5ld\r", i ); 
 
 
 
fread( &pt, sizeof(float), 1, fp ); 
 
 
 
fprintf( df, "%lg\n",pt ); 
 
 
} 
 
 
fclose(df); 
 
} 
 
fclose(hf);

Appendix F – Model Updating Files

This appendix includes the commando files for FEMTools used to performed the updating. 
 
Reduction of The Springs Stiffness – Command Script 
# SAP2000 Updating Base Model No Basement 
# ===================== 
 
# Initialization 
clear all 
 
# Define the test data 
input test format uff file 'OMA_SSI_UPC_modes_shapes.uff' 
transform test tx 4.02 ty -11.113 
 
# Open the SAP model 
sap backup file 'BaseModelNoBasement_spring_reduction.sdb' 
sap launch hide off 
sap open file 'BaseModelNoBasement_spring_reduction.sdb' configure on 
 
# Dynamic analysis solve the model 
dynamic vector 90 
 
# Modal Correlation 
pair tolerance 1.0 node all 
pair mac 50 mode all #50 % mac

# Define the parameters 
#1 
parameter generic solver 'sap' name 'Tower core walls from level P6 to level 5' object 'isotropic' 
property 'e' scatter 1 value 33000000 LOWER 80 UPPER 120 
#2 
parameter generic solver 'sap' name 'Tower core walls from level 6 - 9' object 'isotropic' property 'e' 
scatter 1 value 31000000 LOWER 80 UPPER 120 
#3 
parameter generic solver 'sap' name 'Tower core walls from level 10 - 15' object 'isotropic' property 
'e' scatter 1 value 31000000 LOWER 80 UPPER 120 
#4 
parameter generic solver 'sap' name 'Tower core walls from level 16 - 20' object 'isotropic' property 
'e' scatter 1 value 31000000 LOWER 80 UPPER 120 
#5 
parameter generic solver 'sap' name 'Tower core walls from level 21 - 24' object 'isotropic' property 
'e' scatter 1 value 31000000 LOWER 80 UPPER 120 
#6 
parameter generic solver 'sap' name 'Tower core walls from level 25 - 29' object 'isotropic' property 
'e' scatter 1 value 31000000 LOWER 80 UPPER 120 
#7 
parameter generic solver 'sap' name 'Tower core walls from level 30 - 32' object 'isotropic' property 
'e' scatter 1 value 31000000 LOWER 80 UPPER 120

dynamic vector 10

pair tolerance 1.0 node all 
pair mac 50 mode all #50 % mac

set sensitivity perturbation 0.1 
response frequency 1 to 10 
response mac 1 to 10  
 
# Updating 
set sensitivity perturbation 0.1 
set tune pmax 2 
set tune pmin 0.001 
tune iter 6 eps1 0.00001 eps2 0.00001 #max 100 iterations
